LIAONNG ENVONMENT PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:         People's Republic of China
Bieneficiaries:   1Liaoning Province (LP) and the Municipalities of Anshan, Bertxi,
Dalian, Fushun, Jinxi and Jinzhou.
Arovnt:            $110 million equivalent
Terms             20 years including 5 years of grace, at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate.
Onlending Terms:  From the Government of China to LP for 15 years including
5 years of grace at 70 percent of the Bank's standard variable
interest rate, and a commitment charge of 0.75 percent. The
foreign exchange risk would be borne by LP. From LP to the
Municipalities: on the same terms and a commitment charge of
0.75 percent. From the Municipalities to implementing public
utilities: 15 years, including 5 years of grace, at a rate equal to the
Bank's standard variable interest rate and a commitment charge of
0.75 percent, the utilities bearing the foreign exchange risk.
From  the Municipalities to iadustrial enterprises:  10 years
including 3 years of grace at a rate equal to the Bank's standard
variable interest rate plus a spread of 1.5 percent per annum and a
commitment charge of 0.75 percent, the enterprises bearing the
foreign exchange risk.
From LP to the Liaoning Environment Fund (LEF): 15 years
including 5 years of grace at the Bank's standard variable interest
rate and a commitment charge of 0.75 percent, LEF bearing the
foreign exchange risk.
From LEF for eligible pollution control subprojects: from three to
five years, including one to two years of grace, at a rate equal to
the Bank's standard variable interest rate plus a spread of 1.5
percent per annum on foreign currency subloans and a commitment
charge of 0.75 percent, the enterprises bearing the foreign exchange
risk.

Project Description: The project would support a sustainable environmental setting for
the long-term economic and social development of the Province,
through the provision of urban infrastructure, services and
management improvements. The project would assist in financing
an investment program addressing: (a) deficiencies in wastewater
treatment in Anshan, Benxi, Dalian and Fushun; (b) improvements
in solid waste collection and disposal and water conservation
management in Dalian; (c) air pollution mitigation measures in
enterprises, including improvements in district heating, in Benxi;
(d) the shortages and poor quality of water supply in Jinzhou and
the pollution load emanating from the Jincheng General Paper Mill
in Jmzhou; (e) environmental rehabilitation and reconstruction of
the Liaoning Provincial Museum in Shenyang, and of historic sites
in Niuheliang, Jieshi and Jiumenkou. The project would include an
environmental protection fund to assist in funding eligible pollution
control subprojects of industrial enterprises, and enhance
institutional development through technical assistance and training.
3Beneft:          The proposed project would significantly mitigate environmental
degradation and result in both economic and social benefits for
Liaoning Province, an area of serious and concentrated water and
air pollution, largely of industrial origin. Project components have
been designed to achieve both pollution reduction and improvements
in operational efficiency through technological upgrading, waste
minimization and reuse. Water pollution control at industrial sites
would translate into reduced costs for water treatment and urban
water supply. Health benefits would accrue from the reduction of
waterborne diseases while reduced emissions of toxic gases into the
atmosphere would decrease respiratory ailments and cancer and
reduce corrosive damage to buildings. The project would also
improve operational efficiency in solid waste collection and
disposal. Finally, the project would contribute to the sustainability
of environmental management in the Province through the
demonstration of cost-effective pollution control measures,
strengthening of local capabilities in the monitoring and
enforcement of environmental regulations, corporatization of the
sewerage utilities and the establishment of a sound cost recovery
policy for these pollution abatement services.
Risks:             Project risks include insufficient commitment of the Liaoning
Provincial Government (LPG) and the participating municipal
governments to pursue the pricing policy reforms and institutional
changes required, and to provide the necessary counterpart funds
for implementing the project. Project design has been structred to
minimize these risks. Measures include technical assistance to meet
economic and financial objectives, and assurances from LPG for the
timely provision of counterpart funds.

1.               ONMENAL SECTOR 1
A. ENVRowP4wTAL CONDmTIONS IN CEINA
1.1        Chinese cities have been undergoing remarkable growth and transformation
since the early 1980s, reflecting trends in the economy at large. After two decades of
stagnation, urban population and incomes have been incraing along with industrial
production. The urban population has grown by about 5 percent a year during the 1980s,
and now represents about 30 percent of the total poulation of China. However, the
growing urban population and industrialization are causing widespread pollution in China,
and envimnmental problems have become 4 central issue in Chinese cities. Water
pollution poses the most severe problem and directly affects human health. Many
importnt water resources, which are already scarce in most regions, have been seriously
threatened. Further, few countries are as dependent on coal as China, with annual
consumption of about 1 billion tons. The combustion of bituminous coal is causing serious
atmospheric pollution from airorne particulate and sulfur dioxide emissions in many
industrial areas. Moreover, growing toxic/hazardous wastes are not effectively controlled
and are emerging as a major environmental problem. In the absence of adequate
environmental management progrms, increasingly adverse environmental degradation has
had serious rAmifications, including health hazards and undermining of sustainable
development in the longer run.
1.2        The Chinese authorities are aware of the need to address environmental
problems and recent official policy calls for economic development to proceed in tandem
withi envronmental protectior. The Government's good intentions notwithshtnding, there
remains considerable scope for improvements in integrating environmental management
effectively into macroeconomic policies and economic development plans. In this
connection, there are ample opportunities for economic and enterprise reforms as well as
sound investments that meet both environmental and economic objectives, as exemplifed
by the proposed project. However, there are also inporthnt instances in which economic
and environmental objectives conflict to some extent. In particular, increased energy and
raw mateial production are both a corollary of economic growth and a major cause of
pollution. Further, some of the priority cleanup programs are, by nature, financially
unproductive. As a result, environmental protection objectdves are often compromised by
the scarcity of natural and financial resources. Thus, the Chinese authonties are ficed
with the challenge of formulating a coherent development strategy that would strike an
I/  For a detailed discunion, plem seo 'Chi: EnvironmtW Strateg Pper  ot No. 9669-CH,
Apri 1992).



- 2 -
appropriate balance between the two often-conflicting objectives of economic development
and environmental protection.
B. lISTUTnmONAL FRAMEWORK
1.3        Environmental   gement Arrangements. The institutional framework
for environmental management in China provides a solid foundation for designing
approaches for dealing with environmental and ecological problems.  The State
Environmental Protection Commission (SEPC), under the State Council, includes the heads
of aU relevant ministries and agencies and is at the apex of decision-making on
environmental policy in China. SEPC provides policy direction and resolves interagency
differences, but meets only quarterly so relies heavily on its secretariat, the National
Environmental Protection Agency (NEPA), for day-to-day environmental pcicy
development and implementation. NEPA, which was established in 1979, shares authority
with other agencies for specific natural resotxces. NEPA hlaises with the environmental
protecdon units witiin most of the ministries and state entexprises, and sets the overall
policies and regulatons governing provincial and municipal environmental protecti,n
bureus (EPBs). Annex 1 presents the institutional framework for environmental
potection in China.
1.4        Enviromental Policies.  Most environmental protecdon polcies are
implnemented at the subnational level. Provinces are responsible for implementing national
policies and are authorzed to act in the absence of preemptive national standards; where
warranted by local conditions, they may also impose more stringent standards than those
required by the national government. In all provinces and municipalities and in most
counties around the country, EPBs have been established under local environmental
commissions headed by a deputy governor or deputy mayor. The municipal, urban distict
and county EPBs are the basic units responsible for ensurng compliance with
environmental regulations and standards within their respective jurisdictions. They also
collect pollution charges from enterpnses, except those owned by the Provincial or Central
Govements, from which the pollution charges are collected and used by the provincial-
level EPBs.
1.5        Several Central Government ministries are concerned with water quality and
water pollution control, amid they support NEPA in carrying out its environmental
protection work. As an example, the Nfinistry of Water Conservancy is responsible for
determining policies on the allocation and management of surface water resources in the
country. Its policies are implemented through river basin commissions, provincial water
conservancy departments and water conservancy bureaus at various local government
levels. The Ministry of Geology and Minerals and the corresponding local government
departments and bureaus are responsible fe. allocation and management of groundwater.
The Ministry of Construction is responsible for urban and regional planning, and
infrastructure development planning at the national level. It provides guidance to the
provinces on land use planning and infrastructure development, including urban water
supply and sewerage. The Ministry of Public Health is responsible for providing guidance
to local public health bureaus on drining water quality regulations.



-3 -
C. ENVmoNEwr SECOR ISSuEs AND PROLEMs
1.6        Issues In Environmental Management. China subscribes to the 'polluter-
paysu principle of environmental management. As is common in other countries, a
combination of administrative sanctions and economic incentives is used to induce desired
behavior. However, China has consistently underpriced water supplies and wastewater
treatment. Evidence of that comes from analysis of water and wastewater company
finances and from studies, including those in Liaoning Province, that have shown industrial
wastewater streans to be highly dilute compared to those from similar industries elsewhere
in the world. Underpricing leads not only to excess demand and needlessly large water
and wastewater streams, but also to reduced removal efficiencies experienced when treating
dilute wastewaters. LPG has now recognized these problems and vigorously supports
municipal efforts to raise water supply and wastewater user fees to better reflect economic
costs.
1.7         The national govemment has moved aggressively to ensure that new projects
meet pollution control standards. Specific compliance measures include environmental
impact assessments (EAs) and the requirement that project designs include measures to
mitigate identified impacts, and incorporate either produiction processes or end-of-pipe
treatment that holds emissions within standards. The latter requirement does not provide
direct incentives for enterprises to choose process modification or product design solutions
over end-of-pipe treatment to minimize pollution, and the former approaches often remain
unexploited. A weakness of the current EA system is the lack of a requirement to present
and analyze altenatives to the proposed project. This leaves open the possibility that
superior locations or processes have been overlooked at the design stage.
1.8         Existing enterprises are required to meet the same discharge standards as
new enterprises; however, an altemative to discharge reduction has been the option of
temporarily paying a fee for overstandard discharges. Because the present fee is set
considerably below the marginal cost of tffluent treatment for the bulk of affected
enterpnses, those enterprises would often pay the fee rather than invest in treatment
facilities or cleaner production technologies. Even if technological upgrading has been
identified as the preferred solution, credit constraints may impede implementation. Indeed,
for exisdng polluters, credi* constraints and lack of access to low-waste technologies have
been identified as major hindrances to accelerating the pace of pollution abatement.
1.9         The fee payments for overstandard discharges are the major source of funds
for the environmental protection system. Local EPBs retain approximately 20 percent of
the levy to cover operating costs and make the remaining funds available to polluting
enterprises to finance abatement. These funds are either granted or made available on
concessional loan terms, and the demand for them far exceeds the amounts avaiale at
current levels of fee charging and collection.
1.10        The Government recognizes the need to emphasize clean technologies in
preference to end-of-pipe treatment for pollution abatement. They also recognize that
enhanced formulation, monitoring and enforcement of the pollution levy system would



- 4 -
provide important economic incentives for abatement. In the latter connection, NEPA is
undeaking a review of the levy system, assisted by international consultants funded by
the Bank-supported Environmental Technical Assistance Project (Credit 2522-CHA).
1.11       Design Parameters for Future Policy and Investment lIitiatives. Much
needs to be done to improve the environment, but financing constraints would probably
impede pollution control for at least a decade. Therefore, the real issues are: (a) how to
increase mobilization of resources for the sector; (b) how to utilize the scarce resources
efficiently and effectively to address priority problems; and (c) how to improve
(i) environmental policy, (ii) the economic system and (iii) the government bureaucracy
to maximize pollution prevention and exert greater control.
D. GOVERNMENT STRATEGY
1.12       In recent years, the Central Government and local authorities have taken
greater initiatives to improve the environment.  Since 1979, China has enacted a
comprehensive set of environmental laws, regulations and standards. The framework
established through the Environmental Protection Act of 1979 and subsequent regulations
provides for environmental quality standards for different types of areas, pollution
discharge standards and levies for effluent exceeding the standards, and environmental
impact assessments and permits for most investment projects. Water and air pollution
control regulations were introduced in China in 1979 and 1988, respectively; the Chinese
standards for water and air quality are contained in the Project File. Solid waste
legislation is still in the draft stage, while legislation pertaining to industrial and hazardous
waste management is incomplete and too recent to be effectively enforced.
1.13       The Chinese Environmental Protection Law and ancillary regulations
emphasize "prevention first" (including requirements for environmental assessments-EAs
on new projects) and "polluter pays principle" (including fees, fines and compensation
payments). With a view to achieving its environmental goals, the country has adopted an
eclectic assortment of command-and-control and incentive methods. Command-and-control
approaches include EAs; integration of environmental protection measures at project
design, construction and operational stages (the "three simultaneous actions" system);
relocation of industry away from densely populated urban areas; requirements for low-
polluting technologies in selected industries; centralized treatment of wastes; vehicle
inspection programs; and a system of responsibility contracts (with both voluntary and
binding agreements with industrial enterprises and municipalities to achieve environmental
targets). Economic incentive approaches include a pollution levy system (the principal
method); taxes on polluting inputs; and tax incentives. In addition, a system of standards
and permits is the basis for the pollution monitoring and control program.
1.14       Recent urban growth and industrialition are a direct reflection of the
econonic and institutional reforms being pursued by China. Recognizing environmental
improvement as an essential part of economic development, the Government has
established decentralized institutional and financial frameworks and sanctioned large
investments in the sector. While public revenues consist predominantly of general tax



- 5 -
revenues shared between the cental and local govemments, the Government recently
introduced several new taxes and charges that local govemments can fully use for
environmental investment and operations, and has also been encouraging increases in user
charges and other means of financing environmentally-conscious urban services.
1.15       Under the current decentralized system, the Central Government limits its
role largely to guidance and interregional coordination, giving local governments great
latitude to manage local affairs.  Subject to tax-sharing arrangements and annual
consultation, the local governments are largely free to allocate resources according to their
priorities. NEPA and the Ministry of Construction provide policy and technical guidance
for local governments on infrastructure and environmental issues and sanction major
investment projects. Even on technical and regulatory matters, the central agencies no
longer set ngid norms but allow local agencies to adapt guidelines and standards to local
circumstances. The Central Government, in fact, encourages thiem to take initiatives and
experiment with new technical and managerial solutions, not only to allow local adaptation
of national guidelines but also to find 'best practices through diverse experimentation and
disseminate them in other parts of the country. The reforms and experiments promoted
include increases in public service tariffs, and loan financing for pollution reduction
investments.  Various innovations and large-scale investments for environmental
improvement have been pursued most actively in coastal areas, consistent with the Central
Government's development strategy that identifies these areas as the forefront of economic
growth and interaction with the rest of the world.
E. BANK GROUP OPERATIoNS iN T   SEOR
1.16       The Beijing Environmental Project (Credit 2312-C/Loan 3415-CHA) and
the Ship Waste Disposal Project (Credit 2391-CHA/Global Environmental Facility, Grant
No. TF28613) were the first two free-standing environmental operations in China. The
Bank Group is currently mvolved in eight projects in the water supply/sanitation sectors
in China that also bear on pollution control. These projects, which are now being
implemented satisfactorily, are the: (a) Shanghai Sewerage Project (Credit 1779-CHA/
Loan 2794-CHA); (b) Medium-sized Cities Development Project (Credit 2201-H/Loan
3286-CHA); (c) Liaoning Urban Infastruct   Project (Credit 2219-CHA); (d) Tianjin
Urban Development and Environment Project (Credit 2387-CHA); (e) Changchun Water
Supply and Environmental Project (Credit 2457-CHA); (f) Rural Water Supply and
Sanitation Project (Credit 2336-CHA); (g) Southern liangsu Environmental Protection
Project (Loan 3582-CHA); and (h) the Zhejiang Multicities Development Project (Credit
2475-CHA). Despite the complexity of these ongoing projects, and the initial unfmiliart
of project officials with some Bank Group procedures, implementation has proceeded
satisfactorily; the recently-approved Environmental Technical Assistance Project (Credit
2522-CHA) would assist in strengthening central environmental management capacity. The
Shanghai Environment Project, which would support environmental protection intiatives
by the Shanghai Municipality, was approved by the Board in March 1994; other similar
projects are currently under preparation in Hubei and Zhejiang Provinces and under
discussion in Yunnan Province, as is a follow-up sewerage project in Shanghai. Our
ongoing dialogue in this sector is expected to remain productive and open.



-6-
1.17       Ihe ongoing Liaonini, Urban Infrastructure Project is being implemented
successfully. Tbough behind schedule, the project infrastructure investment is being
satisfactorily managed and constructed.   Although delays were experienced in
implementing project financial improvements, including increases in water tariffs, however,
these difficulties have now been largely resolved and the Liaoning Provincial Government
(LPG) has demonstrated commitment to active financial improvements.
1.18       The common elements of good practices learned under the above-mentioned
projects include: (a) commitment of the concerned municipal authorities to implement the
projects; (b) strong project management offices, with adequate staff to provide the requisite.
coordination among multiple agencies; (c) thorough project preparation based on detailed
feasibility studies prepared by competent agencies and consultants; and (d) well-prepared
bidding documenits and technical specifications. On the negative side, the Bank has noted
a tendency for China and its local governments to postpone the implementation of effective
cost recovery policies, and for the need to adopt long-tern plans for the provision of
essential urban environment services. The proposed project would address these issues.
F. BANK GROP ROLE MD SECTOR LENDIG STRATWY
1.19       The Bank Group's Country Assistance Strategy, presented to the Board in
August 1993, highlighted the need to support economic reform while assisting
infrastructure modernization in an environmentally responsive manner. The Environmental
Stra   Paper (Report 9669-CHA, April 1992) concluded that Bank environmental
assistance to China should focus on long-term protection and resource conservafion
programs, strengthening sector management and supporting financially efficient least-cost
environmental investments. It found that most environmental and pollution problems are
best aodressed at the municipal and regional level because individual sources of
environmental degradation and, therefore, appropriate solutions, would vary. These levels
of government correspond to the principal centers of economic investment decisions and
the focal points of China's environmental policies and institutions. Thus, it is at the
municipal and regional levels that the Barnk's comparative advantage can be used most
effectively. As industry is by far the main source of pollution in the country, the Chinese
authorities have accorded the highest priority to environmental protection in targeted
industrialized cities and regions, including in the major cities of Liaoning Province (LP),
where the proposed project is located.
1.20       Environment-related sector work carried out by the Bank has included:
(a) an urban sector survey in Liaoning Province in 1985, in cooperation with the United
Nations Development Programme (UNDP) and the Australian International Development
Assistance Bureau (AIDAB); (b) an urban sector study in Zhejiang Province (Report No.
6612-CHA), August 3, 1987; (c) a study of the efficiency and environmental impact of
coal use in China (Report No. 8915-CHA), March 20, 1991; and (d) the Environmental
Strategy Paper for China (Report No. 9669-CHA), April 1992. That paper identified
major environmental issues and their underlying causes, assessed China's policies and
institutional capabilities for addressing them, recommended areas for improvement, and
proposed a multiyear program of environmental assistance. The latter included a series



- 7 -
of environmental operations, including the proposed project. Work is almost complete,
with Norwegian Government assistance and in collaboration with the central and the Hubei
and Yunnan provincial authorities, on an urban environmental services sector study, which
would strengthen our sectoral understanding and underpin future lending.
1.21       In addition, various environmental studies are in progress: (a) three studies
under the Global Environmental Facility related to (i) the reduction of greenhouse gas
emissions; (ii) loss of biodiversity in China; and (iii) ozone-depleting substances; (b) five
national studies under the Japanese Government-financed technical assistance program to
NEPA, covering (i) the environmental impact assessment process; (ii) waste minimization
and pollution control in rural industries; (iii) hazardous/toxic waste management;
(iv) economic/financial mechanisms for environmental management (para. 1.9); and
(v) environmental information management systems; and (c) a major Japanese Government-
assisted, World Bank-managed study of environmental issues in the Hangzhou Bay and
adjacent Zhoushan fishing grounds is being undertaken jointly by Zhejiang and Shanghai,
supported by international and local consultants, in coordination with NEPA. In addition,
under the Metropolitan Environmental Improvement Program financed by UNDP and the
World Bank, Beijing Municipality regularly exchanges information on environmental
management and technology with four other Asian member cities.
1.22       Bank participation in the environment sector in China would continue to
focus on: (a) developing appropriate strategies for improving and maintaining the quality
of the environment (these would seek to optimize total benefits, including those from
opportunities to improve the efficiency of industrial production and the use of natuial
resources); (b) prioritizing the measures needed for environmental improvement,
identification and preparation of plans for the most cost-effective investments; (c) financing
high priority investments that would produce environmental benefits in the short term; (d)
strengthening the institutions responsible for enforcing environmental protection
regulations, as well as those that operate community systems for environmental pollution
abatement; and (e) promoting sound financial and cost recovery policies to sustain the
operation of pollution abatement services.



- 8 -
2. PROJECT BACKGROUND
A. PROJEcr BACKGROUND
2.1         In 1986 a survey of conditions in cities in the northeast C-hirnese Province
of Liaoning was conducted under the joint effort of the AIDAB, the UNDP RAS 81/001
Project and the World Bank. The survey identified water supply, wastewater management,
water and air pollution control, and urban transport as major problem areas. Further work
was then carried out by LPG, assisted by consultants financed under an A$4 million grant
from AIDAB to formulate strategic plans for water resource development, water and
wastewater management, water pollution control and urban transport improvement for
cities in the basins of the Hun and Taizi Rivers, which contain the heaviest urban and
industrial concentration in the Province.l/ The cities included Shenyang (the capital,
with a population of 3.25 million), Anshan (1.09 million), Benxi (0.68 million), Fushun
(1.08 million), Liaoyang (0.43 million), and Yingkou (0.36 million). The execution of this
work was supervised by the World Bank Group under a memorandum of understanding
with AIDAB. In addition, LPG also concurrently conducted water supply and pollution
control studies for Fuxin (population of 0.55 million) and Dandong (0.45 million), two
additional important provincial cities located in the Daling and Yalu River basins,
respectively.
2.2         Through the above efforts, long-term plans have been developed and priority
investments identified for the Province at about Y 4.5 billion (in 1988 prices), or about
$1 billion (at the then prevailing rate of exchange) for water supply, sewerage, and water
pollution control works; and Y 1.25 billion, or $0.27 billion, for urban transport works,
as well as institutional development work needed to strengthen these subsectors. Because
of the size of the plans, and the time needed by the Province to plan and implement the
required investments, the works would be implemented over a period of about 15 years.
The Liaoning Urban Infastructure Project (para. 1.17) represents the first investment
phase. It consists of the most urgent works and institutional measures for the improvement
of water supply in the cities of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingbou, and urban transport
investments in Shenyang. The project also initiates efforts to address industrial water
pollution problems. Project implementation is scheduled to be completed by December
1995. The proposed project represents the second phase of identified priority investments,
building on the experience of the first phase to achieve the long-term development goals
of the Province.
J/ The Hun-Tain River basins cover about 20 percent of the Province's land area, and house about
60 percent of its urban population. About 60 percent of Liaoning's industrial output is conacentd
in six major cities in these basins. Six other cities share another 20 percent of this indial output.



- 9 -
B. PROJECT AREA
2.3          The proposed project area comprises mainly the cities of Anshan, Benxi,
Dalian, Fushun and Jinzhou, with a combined population of about 8.0 million. The
Province has a population of about 36 million, with 25 million classified as urban,2/ and
has important iron ore, coal and oil reserves, enabling it to become China's major base
industry.a/ Liaoning's cities grew at an annual average rate of about 4 percent between
1977 and 1986, peaking at 7.7 percent in 1978, but declining to about 0.8 percent from
1986 onward. The rapid growth rate has been attributable mainly to migration from the
rural areas. With a decreasing birth rate, the net population growth rate for Liaoning's
cities is expected to approach the national average population growth rate of about 0.8
percent in the year 2000. The Province has also a significant agricultural output.4.
Annex 2 provides background information on Liaoning Province and the five cities.
C. EXIs G ENvRONmErAL SrrUATION
2.4          Water Quality. The water quality problems of Liaoning Province arise
from the fact that the Province is an area of limited water resources that are subjected to
a variety of demands, for agricultural, domestic and industrial use. The pressure on water
resources is particularly acute in the Hun-Taizi River Basin area, which occupies less than
one fifth of the area of the Province but supports about 15 million people, more than one
tird of its population. A number of cities in the Basin are major centers of industry,
generating a significant proportion of national industrial output, and substantial increases
in production are planned.
2.5          Such a degree of industrial activity, coupled with the fact that there is
,limited treatment of wastewaters, imposes severe stress upon the water environment.
Water supplies in the Hun and Taizi Basin for both potable and agricultural use are
critically dependent upon the two rivers, either by direct abstraction or use of aquifers
recharged by the river water. River water quality fails to comply with national water-
quality standards for the relevant beneficial uses of the rivers over substantial distances.
The situation is particularly acute in the case of cities such as Shenyang and Fushun, which
are in effect close to the headwaters of the Hun River, the flows in which are controlled
by the operation of multipurpose reservoirs upstream. The effects of pollution from such
2t  Urban pop on refes to tse livingie t   mderhe administeaion of a city or a t . A city
is an area where the establishment of the city administration bas been approved by the State. A town
is an area inhabited by no more than 20,000 residents of whom 2,000 or more are nonagmicultul
population living in the site of the township government, or an area inhabited by at least 20,000
residents of whom 10 percent or more are nonagicultural population living in the site of the township
government.
3/ Liaoning Province produces 7.2 percent of C) -'s gross industrial output, with a gross per capita
output of Y 3,416 (1988), which is about 2.1 .    the national average. In terms of gross heavy
industrial output, Liaoning produces 9.4 percent ,. the national output.
it  Liaoning's per capita arcultura output of Y 595 (1988) is slighty above the national aveae, and
agncultural output constitutes about 15 percent of the total provincial output.



- 10-
cities may be felt by users of the river for considerable distances downstream because of
the restricted dilution. In some cases, the degradation of water resources close to cities
is requiring substitution of supplies by exploitation of more distant resources with
substantially increased unit supply costs.
2.6         Water consumption in all cities is dominated by industrial use, which
typically accounts for about 70 percent of consumption. Discharges of pollution from
industry are frequently associated with inadequately controlled, and therefore highly
inefficient and wasteful, use of water and materials. Although water use and wastewater
discharges are controlled by price (user charges and effluent fees), the charges are not at
a sufficient level to have the required incentive effect.
2.7         Previous water resource and water pollution control studies, principally the
AIDAB-financed Hun-Taizi River Basin Management Study (para. 2.1) identified a
program of works for improvement of water quality improvements largely by provision of
wastewater treatment plants. The proposed project addresses water quality problems in
Liaoning by a combination of wastewater treatment, in some cases coupled with recovery
and reuse of treated wastewater, and water conservation/process improvements by industry.
2.8         Air Quality. There are serious air pollution problems in many cities in
Liaoning, in common with many cities in China, and urban air quality in terms of
concentrations of airborne particulates (TSP) and sulfur dioxide (SO) is low, particularly
in winter. The problems of urban air pollution are derived largely from the combustion
of poor-quality coal of high-sulfur content for heat and electricity generation; inefficient
use of energy; lack of emission controls for TSP and S%2; and the discharge of pollutants
close to ground level, particularly in the case of the domestic use of coal. In many cases,
the problems are exacerbated by the effects of the formation of stable temperature
inversions, which can severely restrict the dispersion of pollutants discharged to
atmosphere and therefore lead to particularly poor ambient air quality.
2.9         Air pollution is most serious in the city of Benxi, a center of iron, steel and
cement production, due in part to its location in a river valley surrounded by hills. As a
result, there are stable temperature inversions that dramatically restrict the dispersion of
the substantial quantities of pollutants discharged. During winter months, the ambient
concentrations of 50 increase by a factor of 10 and greatly exceed even the least stringent
Chinese ambient air quality standards (the Class IH standard-the minimum standard for
protection of human health). Of the order of 7 million tons of coal, much of which is of
poor quality in terms of sulfur and ash contents, are burned annually in Benxi principally
for power generation and by industry. Total emissions of TSP and SO2 have been
estimated at 229,000 and 144,000 tons per year, respectively.
2.10        The air quality problems of Benxi, and indeed of the cities in Liaoning, are
such that they cannot be solved in a realistic time frame and at reasonable costs by
emission controls alone, and it would be necessary to adopt a "cradle to grave" approach
to achieve pollution reductions at all stages of the energy generation process from fuel
sourcing to controls on combustion. The proposed project would support the air pollution



- 11-
control program of the city of Benxi in the form of enhanced energy use efficiency,
improved district heating and emission controls at key industrial plants. In parallel, a
Bank-administered Benxi Environmental Energy Management Study would be carried out,
financed by the Norwegian Trust Fund. The study would address the following:
(a) updating the emission inventory; (b) collecting coal quality data for all coal sources
used in Benxi; (c) reviewing the present coal allocation policy and assessing whether the
quality of cwal supplied to each user reflects both the needs of the user and impact on the
environment; (d) analyzing the energy efficiency of all uses of coal; (e) assessing the
degree to which improvements in thermal efficiency and cleaner combustion technology
could be used to reduce emissions; (f) analyzing the benefits of fuel/energy substitution;
(g) assessing the effects of fuel treatment to reduce emissions; (h) analyzing the costs and
benefits in economic terms of the above reviews/assessments; and (i) developing a
sustinable energy plan for Benxi.
2.11        Waste  nagement. The disposal of municipal solid wastes in many cities
in Liaoning is not carried out to accepted international standards of sanitary landfill, and
there are concerns for the impact of waste disposal in terms of effects on groundwater
quality and migration of landfill gas. In addition, the collection of wastes is inefficient,
involving multiple handling of wastes and transport in vehicles of insuffiA;ient capacity for
the operation to be economic. Although the basic objectives of removal of wastes from
city centers may be met, the present wastes management systems are beset with problems
that cause unpleasant conditions and adverse environmental impacts during both collection
and transport of wastes.
2.12        Of particular concern is the collection and disposal of hazardous wastes in
a largely uncontrolled manner. There have been some attempts at waste minimizaton and
waste exchange, but nevertheless there is frequently disposal of toxic waste materials by
unauthorized means such as sewerage systems.
2.13        The proposed project would address the problems of inefficient and
unhygienic municipal wastes collection and transport and the development of
environmentally sound standards of sanitary landfill with controls on leachate and gas
migration.
D. PROJECr FORMULATION
2.14        Recognizing that industry is the main source of pollution in China, the
Centrl Government has targeted LP for receiving attention to improve its environment.
It therefore approached the World Bank for support in funding a long-term program of
investments in environmental protection in the Province (pamr 2.2). At the same time,
LPG recognizes that the sustained protection of the environment is a prerequisite for long-
term economic growth and considers that appropriate investments in environmental
protection are of the highest priority. Presently however, agencies are required to plan
only up to the year 2000. This tme horizon is considered too short given that the
proposed project investments are expected to be completed by 1998. Therefore,
understandings were obtained during negotiations from LP that implementing agencies



- 12 -
would be authorized and instructed to adopt a 15-year planning horizon for infrastructure
investnents.
2.15       LPG directed the Liaoning Urban Construction and Renewal Project Office-
LUCRPO (para. 4.1), which was set up to up prepare the ongoing Liaoning Urban
Inftructure Project (pam. 1.17), to coordinate the preparation, and subsequently monitor
the execution, of the proposed project. LUCRPO retained foreign and local consultants
and local design institutes for preparing the project. The consultants' services are being
partally funded through the ongoing infrastructure project, the Japanese Government-
assisted PHRD fund, World Bank-supported TCC funding, UNDP support, and with
earlier support from the Canadian International Development Assistance Agency
Incorporated.

3. TEE PROPOSED PROJECT
A. PuoET OweCvs
3.1         The project is part of the next phase of a development program for LP to
improve environmental conditions and management. The project objectives are to:
(a) protect the main water resources in Liaoning Province including the Hun-Taizi River
Basin to allow their sustained economic and safe use for drinking, industrial, and
agricultural purposes; (b) strengthen pricing policies and institutional arrangements for
environment protection, water pollution control, wastewater and municipal solid waste
management; and (c) institute measures for air pollution control and cultural heritage asset
management.
B. PRojEcr DEscRPTnoN
3.2         The project would provide physical works and technical assistance for
institutional development in water supply, wastewater treatment, air pollution control,
wastes management, water conservation and process improvements, and environmental
rehabilitation and reconstruction.  A summary description of the various project
components is given below. A detailed description is given in Annex 3.

C. PROJECr Cosr ETMATES
3.6         The estimated cost of the project, including contingencies, is Y 2,941.8
million ($338.1 million). The foreign exchange component is Y 1,558.1 million ($179.0
million), or 53 percent of project costs. The total financing required, which includes $12.7
million of interest during construction, is $350.8 million (Y 3,052.0 million). A summary
of cost estimates is given in Table 3.1 below. Detailed cost estimates are provided in
Annex .
3.7         Project cost estimates are based on consultants' engineering designs,
reflecting price levels prevailing in December 1993. The unit prices were derived through
a reconciliation of data from the following sources: (a) quotations obtained from
manufacturers and suppliers; (b) prices of goods and works from recent contracts in
Liaoning; (c) construction costs according to prices published by the Central and Liaoning
Governments, adjusted for inflation; and (d) the findings of a recently completed "Cost and
R-ates Study" commissioned by the Bank during project preparation of the proposed
Shanghai Environment Project. Physical contingencies of 15 percent have been applied to
base costs for most components, except for the Jinzhou Water Supply and Fushun
Wastewater components, where 10 percent has been adopted to reflect the confidence level
of the estimated costs of these components. To provide for inflation during project
implementation, the following annual escalation rates have been applied: for local
expenditures (excluding civil works), 12.0 percent for 1994, 9.0 percent for 1995,
8.0 percent for 1996, 7.2 percent for 1997, 6.5 percent for 1998-99 and 6.0 percent for
2000-02; for civil works, 20.0 percent for 1994, 12.0 percent for 1995, 10.0 percent for
1996, and 8.0 percent for 1997-2002; for foreign expenditures, 2.8 percent for 1994-2002.
Project management and engineering overhead costs of 11.5 percent are also included.

E. PRocuREMENT
3.11       Procurement procedures and arrangements satisfactory to the World Bank
would be agreed upon with LPG. All procurement activities would be organized through
Shenyang Machinery and Electric Equipment Tendenng Corporation (SYMEEC), and the
China Machinery Corporation (CMC), who have been contracted by LUCRPO to carry out
the work. Both companies are experienced and competent procurement agents who are
familiar with, and have had recent and relevant experience in international competitive
bidding (ICB) work on Bank-financed projects. The tender documents to be used m the
project would be based on the model bid documents that were prepared by the Ministry of
Finance and approved by the World Bank Group. All works and goods would be packaged
whenever practical to attract international competition. Prequalification of bidders would
be undertaken for all civil works contracts with an estimated value over $6.0 million
equivalent each, and for all goods contracts with an estimated value over $1.0 million
equivalent each, except for pipe materials contracts for which prequalification would be
undertaken with an estimated value over $0.5 million equivalent, and for urgently required
district heating equipment, for which bidders would be post-qualified. Annex S lists the
Schedule of Contract Packages and the method of procurement, and Table 3.3 summarzes
the procurement categories and the expected procurement methods. The contract packages
and the method of procurement have been finalized with LUCRPO.
3.12        Civil Works. Excluding the industrial pollution control subloan component,
there is a total of about $140 million of civil works of which about $51 million
(37 percent) would be procured using ICB procedures. Qualified domestic contractors
under ICB would be eligible for a 7.5 percent preference in bid evaluation, in accordance
with the Bank's Procurement Guidelines, May 1992. ICB procedures would be used for
all civil works contracts of estimated value over $6.0 million (i.e., three contracts). All
remaining civil works are too small, scattered or scheduled too far apart to be packaged
to be of interest to foreign firms. Of the remaining works, about $69 million would be
awarded through local competitive bidding (LCB) procedures. The LCB procedures
recently promulgated by the Government have been reviewed and found satisfactory by the
World Bank Group. Interested foreign bidders would be allowed to bid for LCB contracts.
Direct contracting (DC), of about $5.0 million (not Bank-financed) would be used for
contracts to be made with the local power companies to provide electricity supplies to the
pump stations, buildings and facilities under the project, and for utility services; and LCB
procedures (not Bank-financed) would be used for the Anshan effluent reuse pipeline, part
of the Benxi air qwality management works, and a section of the Jinzhou transmission
pipeline, totaling about $20 million.

would be awarded after solicitation and evaluation tirough international or local shopping
procedures of at least three written price quotations from eligible suppliers. This is
conistent with China Investment Bank (CIB) procedures that the World Bank Group has
reviewed and found satisfictory.

down to 50 percent of its initial value, whichever occurs first. Disbursements for taining,

G. LAM AcQuismoN AND RE SrLmEN
3.20       Five project components would require the acquisition of land and
resettlement of current users. These are (a) the wastewater treatment plant at Anshan;
(b) the landfill site at Dalian; (c) the Malan River wastewater treatment plnt site at
Dalian; (d) Fushun wastewater treatment plant; and (e) land for the water supply facilities
at Jmzhou.
3.21        (a) Anshan. About 18 hectares (ha) of land would be required for Anshan
wastewater treatment plant, all of it being paddy fields. The permit for the land has



- 23 -
already been obtained from the city planning bureau. Since the intercepter would occupy
an existing drainage canal, no additional land is required. (b) Dalian Municipal Solid
Waste). No additional land would need to be acquired for Dalian solid waste operations
over the existing 0.8 kma2 already acquired some two years ago. The proposed transfer
staion would be built on an existing waste-hand';ng site, so no land acquisition is involved.
However, t'e proposed access road to the transfer station may involve a small amount of
demolition. This would be documented in the resettlement action plan (para. 3.27).
(c) Dalian (Wastewater). The Malan River wastewater treatment plant for Dalian would
require 13 ha of land within the existing built-up area. A number of enterprises would be
relocated and continue their operations at new sites including the Dalian Chemical Plant
with about 300 employees. (d) Flushun. About 11 ha of irrigated farmland, currently
under a combination of paddy and maize, would need to be acquired for Fushun
wastewater treatment plant. Another 2.4 ha would need to be acquired along a
3.4-km-long section of the intercepter. (e) Jinzhou. About 17 ha of farmland would be
required for wells, pump station, road construction and treatment plant for the Jinzhou
water supply component, together with a further 99 ha for temporary use. Most of the
land is currently cropland, with no buildings or structures.
3.22        No land requirements are necessary for the Benxi wastewater treatment plant
as the plant would be located on part of a larger area of land acquired by the city over 10
years ago. The proposed coilectors and interceptor would be laid in the watercourse
currently servmng as open sewers and drains. No land would need to be acquired for that
purpose, therefore, and the watercourses would remain as drains for stormwater.
3.23        Major resettlement or land acquisition would not be required. An estimated
603 households would require reemployment, of which only 85 households would require
rehousing; these would be done under well-documented Chinese policy and practices that
were recently reviewed by the Bank and found to be satisfactory. LPG anticipates no
difficulty in carrying out land acquisition and relocation of some 1,036 persons. LPG has
formed a team responsible for this purpose; consultation with the affected persons is well
advanced. Prior to appraisal, a land acquisition and resettlement plan was reviewed by the
Bank and found acceptable. Prior to negotiations, LPG forwarded to the Bank an updated
and acceptable land acquisition and resettlement plan. The Land Acquisition and
Resettlement Plan summary is presented in Annex 7 . Its implementation would be
monitored by LUCRPO. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from LPG that
it would cause the concerned agencies to (a) cany out resettlement ofpersons affected by
the project in a manner and according to a schedule that is satisfactory to the Bank
(b) report on the progress of canying out the resettlement plan using monitoring indicators
satisactory to the Bank, and (c) carry out land acquisition in accordance with an agreed
tine-bound Action Plan.
H. ENvIRoNMENrAL IMPACr ASSESSMENT
3.24        The proposed project would have a positive environmental impact. It is a
Category A project according to the Operational Directive classification. The water quality
of many watercourses would be improved by one or more classes after the completion of



- 24 -
the project. Chinese regulations require the preparation of an environmental assessment
(BA) report at the time of project design. The EA was reviewed by LEPB and the World
Bank and found to be satisfactory; it is summarized in Annex 8. While the project would
remove substantial pollution loads, some possible side effects have been identified and
mitigative measures developed dunng the EA process.  For example, appropnate
arrangements have been made for the beneficial use, wherever possible, of construction
spoil. Similarly, the siting of wastewater treatment plants is such that there would be
minimum impact on living conditions, and the discharges would be to watercourses where
the residual pollution load could be assimilated without significant detriment to local water
quality; satisfactory arrangements have also been made for sludge disposal (see Annex 8 .

and correspondence, etc., and 110 staff-weeks would be in the field, generally comprising
two missions per year. More intensive supervision would be required during the first ee
years of the project, during which time the technical assistance would be made operational

4. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND   LEMENTATION
A. PRoJEer MANAGEMEN
4.1        Tkte Liaoning Urban Construction and Renewal Project Office (LUCRPO),
a provincial project office, was set up by the Liaoning Construction Commission under the
Liaoning Urban Infrastructure Project (Credit 2219-CHA); four municipal level project
offices, one each for the water component in the three project cities, and one for urban
transport in Shenyang, were also established to carry out project preparation and execution.
4.2        LPG has appointed a Leading Group headed by a Vice-Governor to direct
project implementation; LUCRPO has been made responsible for managing project
preparation, coordinating implementation and monitoring execution. Municipal-level
project offices have been established in the project towns of Anshan, Benxi, Dalian,
Fushun, Jinxi and Jinzhou. These project offices have been staffed as appropriate, with
plans for staff additions to match project implementation needs, and direct responsibility
for implementation.
4.3        LUCRPO has interacted well with the various project offices and
consultants, expedited resources to undertake preparation, and arranged for project
preparation work to be completed to the satisfaction of LPG and the Bank, resulting in the
project being prepared satisfactorily to date. LUCRPO would have the following
responsibilities during project implementation:
(a)   Overall project coordination, macro-level project management and
monitoring;
(b)   Project-wide Quality Assurance (QA);
(c)   Progress reporting to LPG and the Bank, including cost management,
project impact and environmental improvement assessment;
(d)  Interagency coordination and procurement support; and
(e)   Sectoral taining facilitation.
4.4        To enable LUCRPO to undertake this work effectively, it has been
strengthened, and the personnel slkll mix adjusted to reflect the needs of the
implementation and construction phase of LEP. During negotiations, assurances were
obtained from LPG that LUCRPO and the participating project municipalities' project
offices would be maintained throughout implementation, with fnctions and responsibilities

satisfactory to the Bank, with competent staff in adequate numbers for the duration of the

4.6         Provincial and Municipal Institutions. LPG is headed by a governor
assistd by five vice-governors, each of whom oversees several commissions and/or
departments.  Various provincial commissions, departments and bureaus provide
supervision and guidance to the municipalities on matters relating infrastructure, planning,
development and operation, including environmental protection works. Organization charts
of LPG and the interrelationship of the project implementing agencies with the provincial
govenmment are given in Annex 10.
4.7         With few exceptions, the organizations of the municipal governments of
Anshan, Benxi, Dalian, Fushun, Jinxi and Jinzhou are essentially similar. Municipal
affairs are * ferseen by a mayor and vice mayors who run the municipal economic and
planning commissions, and municipal urban and rural construction commissions. The
latter commissions supervise a number of bureaus that are responsible for the physical
planning, design, construction, operation and maintenance of urban infrastructure, and for
environmental protection. In addition, several bureaus concerned with urban infrastructure
report directly to the office of the mayor. These include the Finance Bureau, the Water
Conservancy Bureau, the Bureau of Public Health and Public Security Bureau.
4.8         As part of China's, and Liaoning's policy of reforms in the public utilities
sector, LPG, together with its participating municipalities, decided to set up self-
accounting. and self-funding state-owned enterprises to implement the wastewater, waste
management and district heating components of the project. Prior to appraisal, wastewater
companies were set up in Anshan, Benxi, Dalian and Fushun; a waste management
company was set up in Dalian; and a district heating company was set up in Benxi. These
companies are financially autonomous.
4.9         Jinzhou Water Supply Company (JWSC). The company was established
in 1945 as a financially autonomous enterprise.  It reports to the Jinzhou Urban
Construction Bureau. JWSC employs a staff of 1,750 who are responsible for construction
management, production, operation and maintenance, customer service and administration
of the Jinzhou water supply system.
4.10        Wastewater. Until the creation of wastewater companies, wastewater
collection, treatment and disposal were generally provided by the sewerage sections of
municipal facilities management divisions (MFMDs) of the municipal governments,
divisions that reported to the municipal urban construction bureaus (UCBs), which, in turn,
came under the authority of the municipal construction committees. MFMDs are also
responsible for the operation and maintenance of roads, bridges, parks and street lighting.
The sewerage sections did not operate on a financially autonomous basis, transferring,
instead, all tariff revenue collections to the UCB. Funds were consolidated at the UCB
level and allocated to the various sections for operation, maintenance and capital works,
on the basis of budgets prepared and submitted by the sections. Staff from the existing
sewerage sections in Anshan, Benxi, Dalian and Fushun were transferred in their entirety
to the recently-formed wastewater companies.
4.11        Benid hion and Steel Company (BENGANG). The Benxi Iron and Steel
Company was established in 1911. It is a holding company for several activities, but



- 29 -
primarily for the production of iron and steel. It is the second-largest iron and steel
producer in the province, accounting for about 13 percent of provincial production. It has
three production aines with the following production capacities: (a) about 3.0 million tons
a year of pig iron; (b) about 2.4 million tons a year of raw steel; and (c) about 2.15
million tons a year of finished steel products. While plant energy consumption is high by
international standards, the company has in the past been profitable both in financial and
economic terms owing to a high plant capacity utilization factor of over 95 percent, and
low wages.
4.12        BENGANG employs almost 74,800 staff in its iron and steel operations and
related utilities. It is currently implementing its eighth five-year plan of new investments
and modernization of existing assets, costing about $480 million equivalent. The plan
includes investments in environmental protection, virWally all of which are included in the
project [para. 3.3(b) and paras. 14-20 of Annex 31.
4.13        Bend City Dongfeng District Central Heating Center (BDHC). BDHC
was established by Benxi Municipality before appraisal in order to supply heat from clean
sources to the majority of Benxi residents (pars. 14-25, Annex 3!. BDHC's initial staff
establishment was 19, drawn from the municipal government cadre. This new source of
heat would eliminate the consumption of coal in most of the 10,000 individual heaters, 17
district-level boilers and 9 smaller boilers, leading to a significant reduction in ground-level
concentrations of pollutants (SO2 and TSP).
4.14        Dalian Solid Waste Company (DSWC). Solid waste management services
in Dalian have been decentralized to the district level, which is responsible for the
collection and delivery of household refuse and street cleaning to a temporary transfer
station. The Dalian Municipal Environmental Sanitation Division has been responsible for
transferring the wastes from the existing transfer station to the landfill. The division has
been leasing vehicles from the military for this purpose. As part of its strategy for
(a) implementing its proposed solid waste component, and (b) collecting and disposing of
its municipal solid waste in the longer term, Dalian Municipality set up DSWC. The
existing staff of the landfill site were transferred to DSWC. As the new company gains
experience, an assessment of its staffing levels, capabilities and training needs would be
undertaken. The company would be responsible for the financng, construction and
operation of a transfer station, and for operating a landfill. It would also be responsible
for the transportaton of wastes between the transfer station and the landfill, except
industral wastes, construction debris and sludge from wastewater treatment plants. These
would be handled by the originators of such wastes. The company would fund its
operations from the transfer station and landfill tipping fees and charges levied on the four
district-level environmental sanitation departments (for refuse generated from street
sweeping, cleansing of public areas and residences) and from landfill tipping fees and
charges levied on industrial and commercial establishments, and the Dalian Wastewater
Company.
4.15        Jincheng General Paper MIU (JGPM). JGPM was established in 1939 as
a foreign-owned enterpnse. Its assets and liabilities were subsequently transferred to the



- 30 -
Central Government, and later to the Liaoning Provincial Government. In 1985, JGPM
became an enterprise of the Jinzhou Municipal Government. JGPM's product line is
paper, produced from reed. Its subsidiary company, the Industrial Commercial Company,
uses JGPM's by-product-red liquor-to produce a lignosulfonate binder material, which
it s   to industry. At present, much of the red liquor is discharged to waterways, causing
a severe environmental hazard. After completion of the project, the red liquor would all
be made into binder, increasing binder production from the present 50,000 tons to 180,000
tons.
4.16        Uaonlg Environment Fund (LEF). The Liaoning Environment Fund
would be chartered by LPG as an autonomous, nonprofit organization. The organization,
.nanagement and operation of LEF are summarized in Annex 11. The purpose of LEE
would be to provide funds for industrial pollution control. Project selection criteria have
been proposed and generally follow those of similar funds in operation elsewhere in China,
which have been reviewed and found satisfactory to the Bank. LEF would provide
financial support for projects in the form of loans (from the project and other sources) and
gants (from LPG sources), with the latter being given for up to 30 percent of the project
cost, averaging about 5 percent of total financing. Projects would be selected according
to cost effectiveness and financial viability. The principal funding sources for LEF would
be fees levied on pollution discharges, and the proceeds of the Loan.
4.17           tonal Development and Training. As the above-mentioned utility
(wastewater, district heating and solid waste) companies have been recently created, the
project institutional development and training component has been designed specifically to
address the accounting, management information, financial management, utility pricing,
and training needs of the entities in particular through activities supported by Package A
(see Annex 3). A contract has been signed with a firm of consultants for providing
Package A services.
C. STATUS OF ENGNEERING
4.18        Project preparation and design work has been undertaken jointly by
intnational and local consultants and design bureaus, and is well advanced. Work on
final design and bidding documents preparation for the (a) Benxi district heating; (b) Benxi
air pollution (coke ovens and blast furnace); (c) Dalian wastewater; (d) Dalian solid waste;
and (e) Jinzhou water supply components are scheduled to be completed by mid 1994; and
for the remainder of the project components by the end of 1995.

5. FINANCE
A. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
5.1         Since 1978, economic reforms have evolved gradually toward an
increasingly decentralized financial system in China. Most of the industrial production and
prices have become free of government control, and enterprises have to purchase most of
their inputs at market prices. Enterprise reform has become a high priority of the
Government. In this connection, the guiding principles of the recently promulgated
'regulations on transforming the management mechanisms of state-owned industrial
enterprises" are to separate government administration from enterprise management,
increase the autonomy and accountability of state-owned enterprises, and increase
competition and market orientation. The reform efforts to improve economic policies and
enterprise efficiencies are essential for sustaining both economic development and
environmental protection in the longer run.
5.2         In order for the enterprises to assume their increased responsibilities
efficiently and effectively, the strengthening of their financial management is essential.
Toward this end, the proposed project would support the applications of modern
management techniques and human resource develop.nent for the utility companies.
Concurrently, the Swedish Government is supporting the financial strengthening of the
Liaoning Provincial Finance Bureau (LFB).
B. ACCOuNTNG
5.3         Different accounting systems are used by enterprises (autonomous units),
construction units (which execute investment projects) and local government departments,
in accordance with regulations prescribed by the Ministry of Finance.
5.4         Enterprise Accounting. All the state-owned enterprises in China follow a
unified enterprise accounting system on an accrual basis. The accounting framework was
developed in the context of a highly centralized planned economy. With a view to
complementing the ongoing efforts to deepen reforms in the financial sector and enterprise
management, the accounting system in China needed modernization to permit satisfactory
financial management in an increasingly decentralized and market-oriented environment.
As a first step, the general principles of enterprise (the term to include public utilities such
as water, sewerage, solid waste management and district heating) companies accounting
were revised by the Ministry of Finance, and introduced from July 1, 1993. As part of
the reforms, enterprises are required to produce five reports for submission to
Govemment: balance sheet, income statement, source and application of funds, profit
distribution, and details of main business. The first two reports are required to be



- 32 -
produced monthly, the remainder on an annual basis. All enterprise implementing agencies
included in the project would be following the new accounting and reporting formats.
5.5         Government Accounting. The regular government department accounts
reflect expenditures incurred against budget allocations by LPG. Accounting is handled
in a decentralized manner through transfers to subunits from which actual expenditures are
made and recorded on a cash basis. Accounts are kept at all levels, and each subunit
reflects in its accounts the budget allocation it receives from the unit above.
5.6         Project Accounts. Project accounts would be maintained by LUCRPO in
accordance with the State Construction Unit Accounting System. This system sets out
detailed accounting definitions for capital works, cash control, receivables, payables, and
specific rules for capitalizing and expending of various items. It also identifies a number
of basic monthly, quarterly and annual reporting formats, including funds flow statements
and balance sheets. The construction accounting system is comprehensive, and is adequate
for the proposed project. Project-related expenditures incurred by enterprises (e.g., the
utility companies), would be reflected within their enterprise accounting systems.
C. AUDrIS
5.7         As with other IBRD-financed projects in China, the Foreign Investment
Audit Bureau of the State Audit Administration (SAA), established in 1983, would have
overall responsibility for auditing accounts concerning the project. The actual auditing
work would be conducted by the Liaoning Provincial Audit Department under SAA's
supervision (to be confirned during negotiations). This arrangement is satisfactory.
During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the following annual audits would be
submitted to then Bank within six months after the end of the financial year: (a) audit of
the project accounts maintained by LUCRPO; (b) audit of the Special Account; (c) audit
of Statements of Expenditure (SOEs); and (d) audits of the financial statements of
r() Jinzhou Water Supply Company; (ii) the wastewater companies of Anshan, Benri,
Dalian and Fushun; (iii) Dalian Solid Waste Company; (v) Jincheng General Paper Mill;
(v) Benxi Iron and Steel Company; and (vi) Benxi District Heating Company.
D. PRovINcIAL FINANCE
5.8         The Liaoning Provincial Government has two types of finance, budgetary
and extrabudgetary. Budgetary revenues are provided through taxes, the base and rate of
which are set by the Central Government. LPG has, however, substantial discretion to
give tax exemptions, and is fully responsible for the administration of the taxes. Most
extrabudgetary revenues and expenditures consist of special fees to enterprises and are used
for capital expenditures. Budgetary and extrabudgetary accounts are not consolidated.
While information on extrabudgetary revenues and expenditures is confidential, and was
not, therefore, provided to the Bank, they are said to be at least as large as the budgetary
data.



- 33 -
5.9         Except for a portion of the environmental rehabilitation component ($1.7
million) and the technical assistance and training component ($10.4 million), the debt
associated with the project is being passed on to the municipalities and their utility
companies and industrial enterprises. LP would monitor municipal finances to assure that
the municipalities continue to meet debt servicing capabilities. However, should the
municipalities and their subborrowers be unable to fulfill their obligations, LP would have
no difficulty in covering debt service through its own resources, as the debt service
represented by the project is minuscule compared to total provincial budgetary
expel. litures. Provincial fiscal budgetary expenditures totaled about Y 24 billion ($2.7
billion) in 1992. During the period 1990-92, revenues and expenditures increased by about
16 percent a year. Revenues are derived mainly from taxes on industry and commerce,
which have been remitted by the municipalities. About 25 percent of provincial revenues
on the average have been passed to the Central Government, and the rest expended on
provincial requirements. Projections of provincial budgetary revenues and expenditures
are included in the Project File (Annex 16).
E. MuNIc&pAL FINANCE
5.10        The municipalities of Anshan, Benxi, Dalian, Fushun, Jinxi and Jinzhou are
expected to provide all funding not met by (a) the proceeds of the Bank loan, and (b) any
funds generated internally by the participating implementing agencies. During project
implementation, the financial projections show that these requirements would be of the
order of Y 205 million for Anshan, Y 546 million-Benxi, Y 379 million-Dalian, Y 238
million-Fushun, Y 5 million-Jinxi, and Y 187 million-Jinzhou.
5.11        Summarized historical and projected revenue and expenditure statements of
the municipalities of Anshan, Benxi, Dalian, Fushun and Jinzhou for the period 1990-2002
are given in Annex 12. The complete statements are included in the Project File
(n&m 1). Project-related expenditures as a weighted average percentage comprise about
3.1 percent of the municipalities' total expenditures, and 14.1 percent of their capital
expenditures. It is concluded that the proposed project would be affordable to the project
cities. Data for Jinxi Municipality are not provided owing to the late inclusion of the
Environmental Rehabilitation and Reconstruction component. However, as their portion
of this component represents about 0.5 percent of both project costs and total municipal
expenditures, it is considered to be affordable to Jinxi Municipality. During negotiations,
assurances were obtainedfrom LPG that it would provide, or cause the municipalities to
provide the necessary counterpart fiunds for the execution of the project.

Water Supply
5.13         Jinzhou Water Supply Company. JWSC made surpluses in 1991 and 1992
of Y 1.2 million and Y 7.3 million, respectively, after making contributions (80 percent
of net income) to a "water resources construction fund" for the financing of its future
(post-project) capital investment requirements. Projections made up to the year 2002 show
that JMWC would continue to post surpluses if it is permitted to maintain the relatively
high tariff setting policy that has been in place from at least the beginning of the decade
(I'able 5.2).
5.14         There were two tariff increases during 1993.  On May 1, 1993, the
industrial tariff was increased from Y 0.59 to Y 0.84 per m3. On July 1, 1993, the
domestic tariff was increased from Y 0.30 to Y 0.50 per m3, and a capital constnrction and
ser ice industries tariff of Y 1.54 per m3 was introduced. The latter tariff, and the
industrial tariff, include a water resource fee of Y 0.04 per m3, which is paid to LPG.
From July 1, 1993, JWSC is also authorized to collect and retain (a) a stepped "excess'
water charge, based on industries' consumption in excess of 90 percent of their 1992
usage, and (b) a one-time charge of Y 5.00 per in2 of construction area of new residential
and office buildings.

Wastewater Treatment
5.16          In 1991 and 1992, the departmental wastewater operations of Anshan, Benxi,
Dalian and Fushun all generated small surpluses. However, as is the practice in
departnental operations, no provision for depreciation was made. Had depreciation
charges been included, all of the operations would have run at a loss. The financial
objectives set by LPG for the newly-established wastewater companies is to achieve full
cost recovery, and, if possible, generate some funds for future capital invesmenet.
5.17          During the construction period the revenue requirements of the wastewater
companies would not ordinarily be expected to cover more than the opeating costs of
collection and disposal of wastewaters. The operating costs of the companies would
increase dramatically, however, when the project-financed wastewater treatment plants
come into operation around 1997-98. In order to minimize the need for substantial
increases in the wastewater tariffs at that time, LPG has directed the wastewater companies
to increase their tariffs gradually during the construction period so that prior to the
commissioning of the plants the companies would be generating revenues sufficient to
cover the costs of treatment as well. The financial projections, which are summarized
below (Table 5.3), take this into account.

District Heating
5.19          Unlike the wastewater operations where financial data are available from
departmental records (para. 5.16), there is no prior experience with heat distribution
operations C(able 5.4). LPG's financial objectives for the company are similar to those
for the wastewater companies, namely to achieve full cost recovery, and, if possible,
generate funds for future capital investment. Following the setting of the initial tariff of
Y 14.50 per en in fiscal year 1994, tariff increases are projected to increase at an annual
average rate of about 16 percent through the year 2001. There would also be a one-time
connection charge of Y 30.00 per ei. The operating ratio and debt service coverage are
projected to be less than 1 and over 2, respectively, throughout the implementation period.
BDHC is projected to make modest surpluses after covering its operating costs.

Solid Waste   agement
5.21         Until the formation of DSWC, the four district environmental sanitation
departments and the Dalian Environmental Sanitation Division performed the collection and
disposal of solid waste, respectively. These units received budgetary allocations from the
Dalian Municipal Government, which were assumed to cover the operations and
maintenance costs of the services provided. Industries paid a nominal Y 1.00 per ton to
the Dalian Solid Waste Supervision Office as a contribution to the management of the
landfill site. As a public utility, the financial objectives of DSWC set by the Dalian
Municipal Govemment are similar to those set for DWC, namely to achieve full cost
recovery, and, to the extent possible, to generate funds for future capital investment. The
tariffs and charges that DSWC would levy on the four district sanitation departments for
the disposal of wastes delivered by them to the transfer station, and the tipping fee to be
levied on industry delivering their wastes directly to the landfill would be expected to
generate revenues to meet the financial objectives. The initial tariff has been set at
Y 24.00 per ton for wastes delivered to the transfer station. The initial landfill tipping fee
has been set at Y 10.00 per ton. The weighted average is about Y 19.00 per ton. The
Y 1.00 per ton landfill management fee paid by industry to the DaLian Solid Waste
Supervision Office is expected to continue. Operational and financial projections of DSWC
are summarized in Table 5.5. In the year 2001, the required average tariff is projected
to be Y 51.5 per ton, equivalent to about Y 31.93 in 1994 prices. The operating ratio and
debt service coverage are projected to remain positive during the project penod.
