G. ENTERPiS
Benid Iron and Steel Company (BENGANG)
5.24        BENGANG operations were apprai, d for both economic and financial
viability. An economic analysis of the production c the major outputs of BENGANG-pig
iron, hot rolled coils, and specialty steel-showed each of them to have good rates of
return. For example, BENGANG's single most important final output, hot rolled, coils,
are produced at an estimated economic cost of $337/ton, while the border price for that
product is $404/ton. Not surprisingly, the analysis showed the critical cost advantage to
come in labor costs per ton of output, while capital costs approximate those of developed
country producers and energy consumption somewhat exceeds that of developed country
producers.
5.25        BENGANG produces consolidated financial statements of its activities. It
has operated with an operating margin of profit of between 7 and 9 percent since 1989,
with profits being distributed between a development fund (for future capital investment),
welfare funds, research and development, and municipal and income tax (50 percent of
profits). With the introduction of an international format of accounts since July 1, 1993
(para. 5.4), staff welfare funds have been incorporated into Salaries and Benefits Expenses,
research and development has become an expense, and the development fund has been
included in Retained Earnings (Table 5.6). The analysis of 1990-92 financial data points
to fluctuating revenues and expenses caused, according to BENGANG, by fluctuations in
the supply and demand for its products. At appraisal, a nominal profit was projected for
fiscal year 1993; the 1993 financial performance was poor, due to unexpected reduction
in sales attributed to the credit limits imposed by the central government in an effort to
reduce overheating of the economy. The intrinsic financial soundness of BENGANG is
considered fully satisfactory however. BENGANG's debt to equity ratio has been low,
suggesting that much of its investment in the past has been financed from its development
fund. Its ability to assume the project-related debt is good, therefore, and the projected



- 40 -
debt to equity ratio averages about 27 percent between 1994 and 1998. Selling prices
would need to increase at about 8.7 percent per year (approximately the average inflation
rate over the project period) in order to maintain a current ratio of at least 1.5; such
increases are feasible, with selling prices below the average border selling price, ensuring
BENGANG's financial viability. Assumptions for the financial projections are given in
Annm  13.

Jincheng General Paper Mill (JGPM
5.27          During the period 1990-92, JGPM  made income before taxes of Y 20.9
million, Y 16.1 million and Y 17.6 million, respectively, on net fixed assets in operation
of Y 180.1 million, Y 168.7 million and Y 157.4 million. This was achieved without any
operating subsidies from government and with sales prices that were fixed by the Price
Bureau. Commencing in fiscal year 1993, its prices have been subject to market prices.
The above income was equivalent to an after-tax rate of return on historic net fixed assets
in operation of 5.2 percent, 4.3 percent and 5.0 percent. The company's debt to equity
ratio was conservative at about 15/8.5.
5.28          While there would be no change in the volume of JGPM's primary product
lines of letterpress, offset and whiteboard, the project investments would, in addition to
improving the environment, assist it to increase its by-product of binder from 50,000 tons
to 150,000 tons annually, which it expects to sell. An additional by-product-man-made
firewood-could also be sold, or consumed in the factory. Modest increases in JGPM's
average sale prices of its primary product lines of about 8.3 percent a year between 1993
and 2001 should enable it to cover its operating costs as before, and service the debt
arising out of the proposed project investment. This approximates the annual general

inflation rate. The current ratio is projected to be at least 1.5 or higher during this period
(Table 5.7). Assumptions to the financial projections age given in Annex 13.

H. ENVRONmENTAL REHABELiTATION
5.30         The Liaoning Provincial Cultural Relics Department (LPCRD) would
implement the Shenyang Museum and Niuheliang historic site subcomponents on behalf
of LPG. The funds would be passed on to it on a grant basis as part of LPG's normal
budgetary allocation. The investment in these subcomponents would represent the
equivalent of about 16 percent of LPCRD's budgetary allocations of approximately Y 20
million a year over the three-year implementation period.
5.31         The Jinxi Municipal Government would implement the Jiumenkou Great
Wall and the Jieshi historic site subcomponents with funds onlent to it by LPG on the
terms outlined in para. 3.9. The investment would represent the equivalent of about
0.5 percent of Jinxi Municipality's total expenditures over the three-year implementation
period, or about 4 percent equivalent of its capital expenditures.
5.32         While entrance fees are charged to visit the museum and historic sites, they
are not sufficient to cover the present operation and maintenance costs. In the case of
Shenyang Museum, for example, the present fees would need to be raised about 15-fold
to cover the current annual costs of operation. Such as large increase would be unrealistic
and, if introduced, would result in few local persons visiting the museum. Options for
direct cost recovery of the investment or incremental operation and maintenance costs
would be developed under the institutional development part of the project. Consistent
with international experience, part of the costs would be covered indirectly through
increased economic activity in the vicinity of the sites, generating additional revenues
through taxes.

6. ECONOMIC, SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL
CONSIDERATIONS
A. PROJECT JUSTFCATION
6.1         The project supports a province-wide combination of innovative policy and
technical responses to environmental problems, as well as investments identified through
earlier river basin and investment planning studies as key to reducing pollution.
Specifically, the project would support:
(a)   a substantial reduction in water pollution in the Hun-Taizi and Daling river
basins and Dalian Bay, thus protecting the environment in LP and the health
of its people;
(b)  the most severely polluted Chinese city, Benxi, in implementing its long-
term air pollution abatement program, particularly in efforts to reduce
ground-level pollution;
(c)   development of the first landfill in the province, that in Dalian, to meet
sanitary standards;
(d)  the city of Jinzhou in its aggressive campaign of managing water demand
through pricing, thereby financing development of new, high-quality water
sources; and
(e)   an innovative program to protect the rich cultural heritage of LP during a
period of rapid economic growth.
B. PRojEcr BENEFrF
6.2         The cost-effective provision of adequate wastewater facilities for Liaoning
Province is a prerequisite to the sustained public health and economic growth of an
important province in the northeast of China. The project would enhance government's
efforts to reform the economy, through the implementation of fiscally rational charges for
water, wastewater, nightsoil and solid waste services. The momentum of economic reform
would be supported by the sectoral breadth of the proposed investments, including water
quality protection, catchment protection, wastewater treatment, waste minimization
measures, and solid waste and nightsoil management; these would provide an enabling
framework for development. Based on the selection of prioritized investments, the project
would remove about 35 percent of the pollutants entering the river system in the project
cities, improve Benxi air quality to a more tolerable quality, and put in place sound



- 43 -
financial prooedures for urban services and management. Details of these impacts and
benefits are discussed below and summarized in Table 6.1. The prqject would put in place
core wastewater infructure to enable long-term environmental cleanup, and solid waft
and air management.

C. ECONOMIC ANALYsis
6.3         The project wastewater treatment components and the Benxi air pollution
control component were selected in accordance with the Liaoning provincial investment
plan to ameliorate major environmental problems and provide the basis foi sustainable
development. The water supply, solid waste, and cultural heritage components were
selected because they support innovative solutions to pressing environmental problems and
as such provide models for other cities and provinces to follow. Project components were
specified in the investment plans to meet performance goals-reduction of water and air
pollution; protection from solid waste pollution; increased water supplies; and cultural
preservation-but that plan did not specify the best means of achieving the goal in the
circumstances of each locality. For several components, especially wastewater treatment,
benefits are difficult to quantify and analysis has been limited to identifying the least-ost
option to meet a performance standard. In others, benefit streams are more readily
quantified and in those cases rates of return have been estimated. The discussion here is
grouped under the categories of water pollution abatement; water supply; air pollution
abatement; solid waste handling; and cultural preservation. However, it must be
recognized that individual project components can address more than one concern, as do
the blast furnace scrubbers in Benxi (with both air and water impacts) or the Anshan and
Dalian wastewater treatment plants (which supply treated wastewater to industry as well
as treat wastewater). The following paragraphs briefly describe the results of the economic
analysis; details are shown in Annex 14.
Water Pollution Abatement
6.4         Three cities were identified as key polluters within the Hun-Taizi basin:
Anshan, Benxi, and Fushun. Under this project all three would, for the first time, provide
treatment for a significant portion of their sewerage flows. Benxi and Fushun would
provide primary treatment and Anshan secondary treatment with sale of the resulting
treated wastewater to the Anshan Iron and Steel Company. Treatment plant placement was
optimized in each city based on long-run master plans to treat or eliminate all wastewater
flows. The new Anshan facility would capture 37 percent of all Anshan flows (another
4 percent is handled in an existing facility) and treat them to provincial discharge standard
II; Benxi 44 percent of flows (with an additional 9 percent being eliminated entirely
through process technology change from wet to dry scrubbing at a steel mill described in
para. 6.8), which would raise Taizi River water quality from Class V to Class IV; and
Fushun 30 percent of flows, which would raise the Shenfu Canal from "out of category*
to Class V and the Hun River from Class IV to m. In each case, altemative plant
placement and size were considered, as explained in more detail in Annex 14. Of the
untreated flows, many have received pretreatment to discharge standards at source (as do,
for example, 28 percent of Fushun flows) and where that does not yet occur, additional
projects over the next decade would provide treatment to meet current standards. As
mentioned, the Anshan facility would provide sufficient treatment to allow the effluent to
be reused in industry. Rapidly growing water shortages in Anshan created the market for
treated wastewater and the estimated economic rate of return (ERR) on the additional



- 45 -
investment needed to treat water for resale is 23 percent. The project would also enable
postponement of future costly water resource developments.
6.5         Dalian wastewater threatens the viability of important coastal fishery and
tourist industries and the project would increase wastewater treatment from the current
6 percent to 44 percent. The project has two components, one of which would enable an
existing treatment plant to produce at design capacity by funding intake sewers and
dewatering and sludge treatment equipment; and the other of which would treat
120,000 m3/day of currently untreated wastewater. The upgrading of the Chunliu plant is
particularly cost-effective, as is typical where projects enable the use of existing facilities.
Dalian is very hilly, which makes centralized sewage treatment difficult. The Mialan plant
would serve one watershed within Dalian that currently discharges to a particularly
sensitive part of the coastline. Both the Chunliu and Malan plants would further treat a
portion of their wastewater for industrial sale. The estimated net economic cost of
treatment is Y 0.57/m3 in Chunliu and Y 0.62/n3 in Malan.
6.6         The Jincheng paper mill, with a 3 million population equivalent COD load,
discharges by far the largest pollution load in the Daling river basin and one of the largest
in the province. Two process technology changes have been identified that would allow
the load to be reduced by two thirds at full implementation. One change recycles water
in the pulp-washing operation. This change has a high ERR, estimated at 37 percent. The
second change would allow expanded production of lignosulfonate binder from waste
streams currently discharged to the Daling. This change has an ERR estimated at
9 percent, where that estimate is based on the cost of alternative techniques for treating the
wastewater as a proxy for averted environmental damage.
Water Supply
6.7        Jinzhou municipality has moved aggressively to control water demand and
ensure funding for new water supplies through increased water tariffs. This project
supports Jinzhou's policy reforms by assisting with funding of one of their new weilfields.
The annex details the economic analysis, which yields an estimated ERR of 12 percent.
Air Pollution Abatement
6.8         The Bank identified, and would support through the loan, elements of the
Benxi long-term air pollution abatement program designed particularly to reduce ground-
level pollution. The city has identified bilateral and internal funding sources for other
program elements. The air pollution control program has three elements. The first
substantally eliminates fugitive emissions from three of Benxi's four coke ovens (the other
to be fitted when rebuilt in 1998); the second expands the district heating program to serve
about 240,000 people, or a quarter of Benxi's core urban population; the third retrofits two
blast furnaces with dry scrubbers that would permit the supply of off-gas for coke oven use
that in turn frees other gas supplies suitable for consumer use, providing gas to 50,000
more households. The latter intervention also eliminates an existing flow of 39,000 m3/day
of highly polluted wastewater.



- 46 -
6.9         The coke oven fugitive emission project was chosen among three technically
feasible alternatives described in the consultant's report. The chosen alternative was the
least-ost means of achieving the EPB-mandated reduction of 98 percent of current fugitive
emissions.
6.10        Benxi proposes to establish a central heating company that would supply heat
from a cogeneration plant, supplemented by high efficiency heat-only boilers. The project
would displace nearly all of the 10,000 individual stoves, 17 of the district-level boilers
and 9 of the very small boilers currently used for heating in Benxi. Particulate reduction
has been estimated at 25,576 tons/year (I1.1 percent of all Benxi particulates) and SOZ
reduction at 8,416 tons/year (5.8 percent of all SO2). This investment was the lowest cost
option for meeting residential air pollution reduction goals.
6.11        The blast furnace scrubber technology change yields an estimated ERR of
6 percent. This lower-bound estimate incorporates the benefits of redvced electricity and
water use in the new technology, the recycling of iron dust, part of the averted costs of
treating phenol and cyanide-ladtn wastewater, and the value of town gas freed for
consumer use. It does not include the full benefit of substantially reduced particulate and
S02 production, nor does it capture the consumer surplus in the switch to town gas.
Solid Waste Disposal
6.12        Dalian lacks, as do all other Liaoning cities, a sanitary landfill; this results
in uncontrolled leachate discharges into groundwater or coastal waters and direct dispersion
into the atmosphere of methane, an important greenhouse gas. Leachate from the present
coastal landfill site contributes to the pollution of Shouyu Bay, the most polluted part of
Dlaian Bay. Other solid waste handling problems include traffic congestion from an
inefficient transfer station and transport system. The cost of the latter problem is high in
Dlian's highly congested traffic network.
6.13        Landfill design alternatives were based on different liner materials to control
and collect leachate, including impermeable clay layers and artificial membranes. The clay
barrier was selected as the least-cost alternative, augmented by a piped leachate collection
system, storage ponds and recycling leachate back into the landfill. The release of
"greenhouse gases' would be reduced by using extraction wells linked to pipes, blowers,
and burners to flare the gas. The system has been designed to allow future integration into
an electrical generation system once gas volume justifies that investment.
6. 4        Transfer station design alternatives are linked to transportation constraints,
in this case of a maximum 20-ton load for trucks. Five altemnative transfer station/
transport models were developed and are fully described in the consultant's report. The
choices represent different combinations of truck size and tipping-floor versus containerized
waste handling, with comp-ction included in two alternatives. For these alternatives, the
average cost per ton handled ranged from a low of Y 22.1 per ton to a high of Y 28.0 per
ton. The selected alternative uses a three-level transfer station with a storage pit, loading
uncompacted waste to 20-ton transfer trucks. The cost of this alternative is estimated at



- 47 -
Y 22.8 per ton, slightly higher than the lowest cost alternative. This choice was made
because, in contrast to the 3 percent less costly, two-level, station, this alternative allows
separation of district waste collection trucks from transfer trucks via a storage pit that can
be isolated to control dust and pests.
Environmental Rehabilitation and Reconstruction
6.15        In Liaoning Province, and in China in general, little attention has been paid
to the physical setting and context of historic structures and to cultural heritage
conservation. As a result, the historic fabric has deteriorated rapidly. As land markets
become more active and property prices in the centers of historic cities increase, the threat
to heritage would continue to intensify. Bureaus charged with heritage conservation have
left largely unexplored the use of financial instruments to fund their activities. At this
critical juncture in the move to a market-based economy, conservation activities would
survive only if a means to maximize revenue generation can be identified. Experience
elsewhere has shown that wouldingness to pay depends largely on offering the public
quality presentation and interpretation of archaeological and historical sites and museums.
Based also on international experience, visits to cultural sites can be expected to rise with
the rapid increases in discretionary income and leisure time now enjoyed in China.
6.16        The project is aimed at furthering scientifically sound, least-cost approaches
to heritage asset conservation. By promoting improved management, conservation, and
fmancial analysis, the project would encourage more efficient allocations of human and
financial resources. The contribution of cultural heritage to domestic and international
tourism in Liaoning would be strengthened by the project activities that address
presentation of heritage sites and museum collecdons. Once these improvements are in
place, pricing policies for heritage sites can be evaluated so as to increase revenue. The
project, in supporting cultural heritage conseriation and in consistency with Bank policy
(OD 4.00), would also underline the importance of this resource to Liaoning and the
nation.
6.17        Project elements were chosen from a broad array of possible activities, with
the selection designed to cover representative conservation and management problems in
museums, archaeological sites, and historic structures. The component has four major
elements, each with associated training: improvement of services for the world-class
collection of the Liaoning Provincial Museum; protection of the Niuheliang archeological
site of the Hongshan culture; protection and presentation of the World Heritage Site at
Jiumenkou on the Great Wall; and site investigations at the Jieshi Palace, reputed to have
been occupied by one of China's greatest emperors. The experience gained from each
activity, reinforced through training provided under the project, would be replicable in
other Liaoning sites.
D. ENVoRONMEnAL IlMPACr
6.18        The project would contribute significantly to improving the environment and
is rated category 'A' in terms of the World Bank's Operational Directive on



- 48 -
Environmental Assessment (EA). Accordingly, an EA has been completed, reviewed by
the Bank and approved by NEPA. A detailed annex on the environment has been prepared
(Anex 8), which summarizes the findings of the EA on each component and reflects the
appraisal mission findings.
E. AFFORDABuJTY
6.19        Water. The present Jinzhou water supply tariff was enacted before project
appraisal and has been effective for some six months without evidence of difficulty for any
part of the population. Projected increases are within the range of projected real income
increases; thus, affordability should not become an issue.
6.20        Wastewater. Because the wastewater tariffs proposed for Anshan, Benxi,
Fushun, and Dalian are linked to water consumption, they would substantially increase the
cost of such consumption. Based on provincial average figures, per capita consumption
of water was 158 liters/Jay in 1992. Assuming that the poorest 5 percent consume
130 liters/day, then no more than an average of about 1.4 percent of their Y 1,005/year
income would be spent on water. Anticipated wastewater charges are well above current
water fees (which are in most cases below the level needed for a positive rate of return on
water company capital), so if those households make no adjustments in their consumption,
an additional 2.2 percent of their income would go to the sewerage tariff. This would
represent, in effect, a significant increase in the cost of water. However, the combined
cost of 3.6 percent of income is not especially high by developing country standards, and
at the postulated consumption levels, room exists to reduce consumption and hence
payments.
6.21        Industry would also face much higher charges for the combined water/
wastewater service. However, research has shown high levels of waste in current
industrial water use. Industries are expected to quickly adopt various water-saving
techniques that have been introduced to China in recent years, thereby mitigating the
impact of these charges on production costs.
F. RISKS
6.22        Management and Sustainability. Proposed revenue and service charge
enhancement, together with the investments to improve water quality and reduce the risks
to Liaoning of major water supply pollution, would provide a framework for project
sustainability. The operations and maintenance costs to support the investments would be
provided through existing revenue arrangements; these would be further streamlined and
enhanced as part of the project. Project management of the proposed widespread
investments would need to be efficiently implemented; technical assistance and training
would mitigate this risk. LPG and the utility companies do not have sustained experience
with operating large-scale wastewater treatment plants. This risk is acceptable, however,
since the project includes a carefully designed institutional development and training
component, which would reduce operating risks.



- 49 -
6.23        Commercial. Three project components, Anshan, Chunliu, and Malan,
recoup part of their operating costs through resale of treated wastewater after treatment.
The failure to sell water, or sell as much as planned, would not change the basic treatment
plant size and siting decisions. It would, of course, affect the decision to add the tertiary
treatment that permits water resale. In the two cities involved, industries have long faced
water shortages and have a history of treated wastewater use. Therefore, techniques and
markets have developed to use this product. Economic growth and fal1ing groundwater
levels are exacerbating existing shortages and bolster demand for treated wastewater. The
risk of not finding a market for the water is therefore considered minimal.
6.24        The profitability of the Jincheng pulp and paper mill red liquor processing
investment depends on being able to sell substantially more binder than currently produced
by the mill. The mill sales staff has a good command of the binder market in China and
has identified buyers for the additional product. Here, the risk appears to be one of
macroeconomic reversals that would suppress demand for the zinc and fire retardant for
which the binder is an input. Such risk cannot be ignored, but the underlying markets
appear strong and reversals, if any, should be short lived.
6.25        Tariff Implementation. Reluctance by LPG to implement and maintain
economically efficient wastewater tariffs would undermine municipal utility company
financial viability: project design requires meaningful tariff action early in project
processing. LPG implemented substantial tariff increases prior to appraisal, thereby
lessening this risk significantly; specific financial covenants agreed at negotiations would
enable effective monitoring of progress.
6.26        Follow-on Investments. To reduce water pollution in the Hun-Taizi and
adjacent river basins to acceptable levels would require considerable follow-on investments
if the long-term environmental benefits were to be maximized. While LPG proposes to
carry out the program in its entirety, this commitment would necessitate diverting funds
from other productive purposes, which may be difficult. However, LPG and the municipal
governments are fully aware of the importance and potential economic benefits of
environmental protection, and the project-generated benefits would be significant even
without these additional investments.
6.27        Operating and Other. The annex describes in some detail the risks faced
by each project component. Operating risks are negligible, due to the familiarity of the
proposed technologies in C1-ina.



- 50 -
7. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1         The following assurances were obtained at negotiations:
(a)   ANGANG would implement an agreed in-plant pollution control and water
conservation time-bound Action Plan [para. 3.5(a)];
(b)   The participating municipalities would implement an agreed time-bound
Action Plan to connect buildings and septic tank and nightsoil facilities that
are within 100 m of their sewerage systems [para. 3.5(b)];
(c)   BMG would implement an agreed time-bound Action Plan for river training
and other necessary works to protect the wastewater interceptor culvert
[para. 3.5(c)(i)];
(d)   BMG would cause the Benxi Gas Company to expand its gas distribution
network to be expanded in accordance with an agreed time-bound Action
Plan to accommodate the additional gas supplies generated by
implementation of the Benxi blast furnace component [para. 3.5(c)(ii)];
(e)   BMG/BEPB would implement an agreed time-bound Action Plan for
wastewater treatment to national discharge standards for the industrial
wastewater discharges not included in the Benxi wastewater component
[para. 3.5(c)(iii)];
(f)   FMG/FEPB would implement an agreed time-bound Action Plan for
wastewater treatment to national discharge standards for the industries not
included in the Fushun wastewater component and discharging to the Shenfu
Canal [para. 3.5(d)];
(g)   LP would carry out institutional development and training in accordance
with an agreed time-bound Action Plan [para. 3.5(e)];
(h)   The municipalities would onlend the loan proceeds to their respective
implementing agencies, on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank
(para. 3.9);
(i)   LEF would onlend to industrial enterprises, on terms and conditions
satisfactory to khe Bank [para. 3.10(a)];

of pollution levy fees each fiscal year [para. 3. 10(b)];
(k)  the project cities and implementing agencies would (i) carry out the

progress in carrying out the resettlement plan, using monitoring indicators
satisfactory to the Bank; and (iii) carry out land acquisition in accordance
with an agreed time-bound Action Plan (para. 3.23);
(1)   LUCRPO and the municipal project offices would be maintained throughout

and with competent staff in adequate numbers for the duration of the project
(para. 4.4);
(m)  the following annual audits would be submitted to the Bank within six

(n)   LPG would provide, or cause the municipalities to provide the necessary

(para. 5.15);
(p)  the wastewater companies of Anshan, Benxi, Dalian and Fushun, the Benxi
Dongfeng District Central Heating Center and the Dalian Solid Waste
Company would, commencing in fiscal year 1995, (i) generate revenues (to
include sales of treated wastewater to industries by Anshan and Dalian

debt serwice coverage of at least 1.3 times (paras. 5.18, 5.20 and 5.22);

(q)   the project utility (water supply, wastewater, district heating and solid
waste) companies would, before September 30, 1995, and in each of th*
following fiscal years, prepare forecasts satisfactory to the Bank, reviewing
whether they would meet their respective requirements in such year and the
following fiscal year, and furnish the results of such reviews to the Bank;
if any such reviews would show that the companies would not meet their
requirements, LPG would cause the companies to take all necessary

(r)   Benxi Iron and Steel Company would maintain the following current ratios:

(s)   Jincheng General Paper Mill would, commencing in fiscal year 1995,

7.2         The following understandings were obtained during negotiations, and
referred to in the minutes of negotiations:
(a)   the implementing agencies would be authorized and instructed to adopt a 15-

(b)   ANGANG would purchase treated wastewater from AWC at a price
sufficient to enable AWC to cover the operation and maintenance costs of

(c)   Jinxi Municipal Government would upgrade the access road to Jiumenkou

(d) LPG would arrange for the construction of a financial training center, using

(e)   LPG would introduce residential wastewater tariffs no later than fiscal year
1997 (para. 5.18).
7.3         The execution of subsidiary loan agreements between the project
municipalities and implementing agencies, satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.9) is a
condition of loan effectiveness.
7.4         Conditions of disbursement for the LEF component are the establishment of
LEF, and the execution of an acceptable financial agency agreement between LEF and a
bank for LEF subloan processing (para. 3.19).

BACKGROUND TO LIAONING PROVINCE AND
PROJECT CITIES
Liaoung Province
1.         Liaoning Province is located in the northeast of China and is bordered to the
north by Jilin Province, to the east by the Democratic Republic of Korea, to the south by
the Bohai Sea and to the west by Hebei Province and the Inner Mongolia Autonomous
Region. The province has a total land area of 145,700 km2 and a population of 36 million
(1992), of which 25 million are urban.
2.          The province has important iron ore, coal and oil resources, which have
enabled it to become the most industrialized province in northeast China and one of the
most industrialized in China as a whole. Liaoning Province produces 7.2 percent of the
gross industral output of China, with a gross per capita output more than double the
national average. Many of the cities of Liaoning are major centers of heavy industry on
a national scale, and the province produexs 9.4 percent of gross national heavy industrial
output. Of particular significance is the steel industry, which accounts for more than
25 percent of national production.
3.          Liaoning also has a significant agricultural output, slightly above the national
average on a per capita basis. Agncultural output constitutes about 15 percent of the total
provincial output. Basic food supplies are derived from agriculture carried out in several
large river basins, principally the twin basins of the Hun and Taizi Rivers in the heartland
of the province. The rivers flow from northeast to southwest for some 415 km before
joining to form the Daliao River, which discharges to the Liaodong Gulf of the Bohai Sea,
some 98 km farther downstream. The Hun and Taizi basins occupy some 20 percent of
the land area, contain more than one third of the population of Liaoning, and produce
about 60 percent and 40 percent, respectively, of its gross industrial and agricultural
output.
4.          The province has very limited water resources, which on a per capita basis
are one third the national average and the third lowest of all the provinces. Historic rapid
industral development, coupled with inadequate wastewater management, has seriously
threatened the existing limited water resources, and substantial increases in industrial
production are planned. The total quantity of wastewater discharged from the six major
cities in the Hun-Taizi Basin have been estimated at 2.4 million m3/d in 1987 and are
projected to increase to 4.4 million m3/d in 2000. In addition, independent discharges
from major industries are estimated to rise from 2.5 million m3/d in 1987 to 3.8 million
m3/d in 2000. Such increases would further degrade the limited water resources of the



- 57 -                         ANNEX 2
province. Water pollution is already reported to be causing significant losses in agriculture
because of the poor water quality in watercourses used for irrigation.
S.          Water consumption in all cities is dominated by industrial use, which
typically accounts for about 70 percent of consumption. Discharges of pollution from
industry are frequently associated with inadequately controlled and therefore inefficient and
wasteful use of both water and materials. Water consumption per unit of industrial output
is frequently very high by international standards. The use of water per unit output of
steel and petroleum have been estimated at up to 5 and 10 times, respectively, the
corresponding use elsewhere.
6.          Studies of diversion of water from catchments where there are, albeit
modest, surpluses to augment or substitute existing polluted resources have indicated that
unit costs are very substantial and in some cases exorbitant. Thus, there is a clear need,
in addition to more effective wastewater management, for industry to improve the
efficiency of water use if industrial growth is to be sustained. Indeed, there is evidence
that industrial output in some cities is already constrained by water resource restrictions.
Potential industrial output lost as a result of water shortages has been estimated to be in
the order of millions of dollars per year.
7.          Similar lack of controls on polluting discharges and the inefficient use of
resources are also causing severe problems of air pollution in many of the major cities in
the province. The problems are derived principally from the combustion of poor-quality
coal (in terms of high sulfur and ash contents) for heating, power generation and industrial
use and result in extremely poor urban air quality, with substantial exceedance of even the
least-stringent air quality criteria, principally concentrations of sulfur dioxide (SO2) and
particulates (TSP). The nature and extent of the problems are such that they cannot be
solved at a reasonable cost and within a realistic timeframe solely by the provision of
emission controls. There would need to be a 'cradle to grave" approach to achieve
pollution reductions at all stages ranging from fuel sourcing to controls on combustion.
8.          Solid waste management throughout the province is inefficient and has
adverse effects on the environment. Waste collection and transport frequently involve
small vehicles and multiple handling of small quantities of waste, and standards of hygiene
are poor. Waste disposal is largely by simple dumping with very limited control, and there
are no sites operated to international standards of sanitary landfill. Wild fires occur
frequently on the dumps and landfills; as a result, there are problems of pollution of air,
surface runoff and groundwater.
9.          The effective utilization of water resources in the Hun-Taizi Basin was the
subject of a previous study, the Hun-Taizi River Basin Management Study (HTRBMS),
managed by the Bank and funded by the Australian International Development Assistance
Bureau (AIDAB). The study was completed in 1988 and produced a ranked set of
investments for water resource development and water pollution control measures designed
to improve water resource utilization in the basin. A number of water resource
developments were included in the Bank-financed Liaoning Urban Iftastructure Project



- 58 -                         ANNEX 2
and the proposed project would finance, inter alia, the second set of priority investments
identified by HTRBMS focused on water pollution abatement.
10.         Liaoning Province has an important cultural heritage that reflects the eventful
history of the region, its natural resource endowments, and its strategic location.
Throughout the province are cultural relics that represent the social and cultural
developments that have taken place in Liaoning. They range chronologically from the
paleolithic Jinniushan cave site, to Hongshan culture sites at Niuheliang (circa 3000 BC),
to the Jieshi Gong palace site identified with Emperor Qinshihuang (circa 220 BC), the
Great Wall at Jiumenkou (a World Heritage Site), the early timber Fenghousi Buddhist
Temple (circa 970 AD), the Manchu Palace at Shenyang (1450 AD), and the medieval
walled city of Xing Cheng. The Liaoning Provincial Museum and the Palace Museum are
noted for their great collections of Chinese paintings, textiles, lacquers, metalwork, and
ceramics. Numerous local museums dnd hundreds of cultural sites of national, provincial,
and local levels of importance are located in the province. The physical environments of
the heavily industrialized cities of the province, where new construction is occurring at a
rapid pace, present grave threats to the survival of this heritage. Conservation efforts and
development control would need to be increased in order to avoid the loss of this valuable
resource.
Anshan
1I.         Anshan is one of the major industrial cities in Liaoning and is located in the
Taizi River basin, and is the most downstream of the major cities in the basin of the Taizi
upstream of its confluence with the Hun River. The population of Anshan was
approximately 1.4 million in 1992, projected to rise to some 1.7 million in 2010. The city
is a major center of the steel industry. The Anshan Iron and Steel Complex (ANGANG)
in the city produces about 40 percent of steel output in Liaoning and more than 10 percent
of national output. Other sectors of industry represented in Anshan include petroleum and
petrochemicals, textiles and paper.
12.         The significance of Anshan as a major industrial center is reflected in the
large proportion of water consumption by industry. In 1990, industry accounted for
81 percent of the total water supply of 1.02 million e3/d. Total demand is projected to
increase to 1.56 million m3/d, of which industry would consume about 78 percent.
13.         The Taizi River runs to the west of the city with three tributaries of the
Taizi, the Nanshan, Yunliang, and Yangliu Rivers, running from east to west through the
city. These river systems and their tributaries in the city are used as wastewater channels,
intercepting all municipal and industrial wastewater from the city area, including discharges
from more than 100 major industries, for conveyance to the Taizi River system. As a
result, the water quality classification of the Taizi downstream of the discharges from
Anshan is Class V of the national surface water quality classification.
14.         The sewerage system in Anshan serves about 85 percent of the city; there
is only a single municipal wastewater treatment plant of capacity only 20,000 m3/d,



-59-                          ANNEX2
providing only primary treatment. In addition, there are industrial wastewater treatment
plants at the ANGANG complex, treating 60,000 m3/d, and at 38 other industries, all of
which are understood to experience difficulties in meeting provincial wastewater discharge
standards.
15.        The overall objectives of water management in Anshan are:
(a)  to improve water quality in the Taizi and its tributaries for use in irrigation
and industrial water supply; and
(b)  to conserve limited water resources by improving the efficiency of water use
in industry.
The long-term (up to 2020) wastewater treatment plans for Anshan involve three major and
two smaller plants with a combined capacity of some 750,000 m3/d. The highest priority
plant is one that would treat largely industrial wastewater currently discharged to the Xida
(3ou, a tributary of the Yunliang.
16.        Benxi is an industrial city, situated in hilly terrain on the upper reaches of
the Taizi River, which flows through the city in a southwesterly direction. The population
of Benxi in 1992 was about 0.95 million and is expected to reach some 1.2 million by year
2010.
17.        Benxi is an important industrial center of Liaoning Province with 230 large
plants covering 25 sectors of industry. The most important sector is the production of iron
and steel. The Benxi Iron and Steel Compsiy (BENGANG) is the second-largest steel
producer in the province, accounting for about 13 percent of production. Other significant
sectors include chemicals, pharmaceuticals, cement, paper and textiles production.
18.        The city is located some 40 km downstream of the multipurpose Guanyenge
reservoir and immediately upstream of the Shenwo reservoir, both of which impound the
mainstream Taizi and one or more of its tributaries. Water quality in the Taizi upstream
of Benxi, from where the city mainly derives its potable water supply, is generally good,
attaining Class II of the national classification. However, as the Taizi traverses the city,
it becomes highly polluted by the discharges from a number of open ditches containing
untreated municipal and industrial wastewater, and immediately downstream of the city the
river water quality deteriorates to Class V. The pollution from Benxi also degrades water
quality in the Shenwo reservoir.
19.        The existing sewer system is basically a combined system dating back for
more than 60 years. Wastewater flow projections indicate combined domestic and
industrial flows increasing from 680,000 m3/d in 1990 to around 1,008,000 m3/d by 2010.
During periods of low flow in the Taizi, the wastewater flow is similar to or even exceeds
the natural river flow.



- 60-NEX 2
20.         The City of Benxi has the poorest air quality of any city in Liaoning and
probably in China. Measured air quality (principally concentrations of TSP and SO)
varies as a function of sampling location and seasonally, and is particularly poor in winter,
when SO? concentrations rise by up to an order of magnitude. Concentrations of S02 and
TSP substntially exceed even the least-stringent air quality standards.
21.         The poor air quality in Benxi is largely a result of the combustion of coal
of poor quality (in terms of high sulfur and ash contents) by industry, for power generation
and heating, and of the widespread absence of emission controls on discharges to
atmosphere. Total consumption of coal in Benxi is of the order of 7.5 million tons per
year and the major emissions of SO2 and TSP are from power generation, iron and steel
production (blast fumaces and coke ovens) and cement manufacture. Total emissions of
SO% and TSP have been estimated at more than 140,000 and 220,000 tons per year,
respectively (1991), the dominant contributor being BENGANG, which, in addition to
producing iron and steel, also provides the electncity and gas supplies for the city.
22.         The air quality situation in Benxi is exacerbated by the location of e tcity
in a river valley surrounded by hills. The formation of stable temperature inversions
severely restricts the dispersion of the substantial discharges of pollutants leading to very
poor local air quality, particularly in winter.
Dal
23.         Dalian is at the southem tip of the Liao Dong Peninsula about 360 km from
Shenyang, the provincial capital. The city is located on a coastal strip surrounded by the
Bohai Sea on the northwest and the Yellow Sea to the southeast. The urban population of
Dalian was approximately 2.3 million in 1990 and is expected to rise to 3.7 million by
2010. The economy of the city depends on transportation, fisheries, tourism and
industries, including metallurgy, textiles, food processing, electronics, and building
materials.
24.         The city derives its water supplies from  two reservoirs and from
groundwater. Total water supply is projected to rise from 424,000 m3id in 1990 to
1,342,000 m3/d in 2010. However, Dalian has among the highest-cost water resources of
the cities in Liaoning, and there is currently significant suppression of demand because of
restricted supplies. This has led to the recovery of treated municipal wastewater for use
by industry in Dalian.
25.        In Dalian, the primary concern with regard to the control of pollution caused
by untreated wastewater discharges relates to the need to protect water quality in the
coastal areas. Heishijiao Bay, with an area of 36 knIm at the mouth of the Malan River on
the southem coast of the city, is important for aquaculture of kelps and shellfish,
principally abalone, mussels and cla..s. In addition, the coastal waters are extensively
used for recreation. Water quality in these recreational waters indicates a significant
degree of bacterial pollution from domestic wastewater. The shellfish production is
threatened by the occurrence of red tides and the concentration by the shellfish of heavy



-61-                           ANN      2
metals derived from industrial wastewater discharges to the Malan River and thence to
Heishijiao Bay.
26.         Dalian Bay on the east coast of the city has an area of 210 kn9 but is
semienclosed, with limited water movement. West Bay, the inshore area of Dalian Bay,
has particularly poor water circulation and is heavily polluted by numerous discharges of
untreated wastewater.
27.         A total of eight wastewater treatment projects have been identified for the
city, sesving a total population of 1.7 million. Only one has been constructed, the Chunliu
plant discharging to West Bay, and this is not functioning at full capacity because of
sewerage system limitations that restrict the flow that can be treated. Part of the treated
wastewater is recovered for use by local industry.
28.         Approximately 85 percent (1,100 tons per day) of the municipal waste in
Dalian is collected regularly. Industry provides its own collection and disposal service;
while the collection coverage is satisfactory, severe littering and pollution problems are
associated with the solid waste management system. The open collection bins cause
littering, bad odor and are eyesores in the urban environment. Collection vehicles bring
the waste to a transfer point at Shouyu Bay, which is near the city center, and dump it on
the ground where more than 120,000 m3 of waste has accumulated, because the transport
vehicles used are too small and inefficient.
29.         Uncontrolled fires in the decomposing garbage and bad odor foul the air.
Polluted water flows in the Shouyu Bay, and loose waste litters the area around the transfer
dump. The waste is transported in open trucks to a landfill at Maoyingzi, where it is
disposed of in an uncontrolled manner on the shores of Jinzhou Bay; leachate pollutes the
coastal waters.  The fumes from  frequent wild fires on the landfill give rise to
environmental and public health hazards.  The landfill gases released from  the
decomposing vwaste also contribute marginally to global warming.
30.         The problems of waste management in Dalian are typical of those of the
province as a whole and are exacerbated by the fact that the principal landfill site is located
some 25 km from the city, resulting in high transport costs because of the small capacity
of the vehicles used. Although there is a basic waste transfer facility at the former Shouyu
landfill site close to the city, the improvement in transport efficiency is limited because
there are no waste compaction facilities.
Fushun
31.         Fushun is located on the Hun River about 50 km upstream from Shenyang
and immediately downstream of the multipurpose Dahuofang reservoir used for flood
alleviation and water supply purposes. The 1992 population was 1.2 million, projected to
rise to 1.5 million by 2010.



- 62 -                        ANNEX 2
32.         Fushun is a major center of heavy industry in Liaoning and has more than
1,100 industrial plants covering 45 sectors of industry. Principal activities in Fushun
include coal mining, metallurgy, petroleum and petrochemicals, and machinery
manufacture. Two large thermal power stations are located within the city. Substantial
increases in industrial activity are planned.
33.         Water consumption in Fushun is dominated by industry, which accounted
for more than 80 percent of the total 1990 wastewater flow of approximately
600,000 m3/d. Almost half the industrial wastewater quantity wvas accounted for by
discharges from power stations and coal mines. Wastewater quantities are projected to rise
to approximately 1.3 million m3/d in 2010, with industry still accounting for 75 percent
in spite of substantial expected economies in water use. Some 40 industries in Fushun
have installed some form of wastewater treatment facility, but the effectiveness of these
in terms of reducing pollution is very limited.
34.         At present, wastewater is discharged untreated into the Hun River, which
runs through Fushun from east to west, and to the Shenfu Irrigation Canal. Since Fushun
is located immediately downstream of the Dahuofang reser-voir, the flow in the Hun River
in Fushun is very restricted, as therefore is the dilution of the wastewater discharges. As
a result, the water quality in the Hun River as it traverses Fushun fails even to comply
with the Class V classification. The Shenfu canal is similarly highly polluted because of
very limited dilution of wastewater discharges. It has been estimated that in the Shenyang-
Fushun area, water pollution has caused a loss in economic terms of about 6 percent of the
local agricultural output.
35.         The primary concerns with regard to the wastewater pollution in Fushun are:
(a)  the need to protect the Shenyang aquifer, which is recharged largely by the
Hun River downstream of Fushun and which provides the Shenyang water
supply; and
(b)  the control of pollution in the Shenfu irrigation canal.
36.         Since the Hun River is the major source of recharge for the Shenyang
aquifer some 20 km downstream of Fushun, the water quality in this section of the Hun
River needs to be improved from below Class V to Class MU. Similarly, the water quality
in the Shenfu canal needs to be improved to Class V to meet irrigation requirements.
Jlnzhou
37.        Jinzhou is situated southwest of Liaoning on the Xiaoling River about 20 km
north of the Bohai Sea, and is located on the major transportation corridor between Beijing
and the industrial northeast of China. The city is an economic and cultural center for
western Liaoning and has an industrial base comprising petrochemical, metallurgical,
electrical, textile and medical industries. The urban population is currently about
0.9 million, projected to increase to approximately 1.3 million in 2010.



- 63 -                         ANNEX-2
38.         The municipal water supply for the city is supplied from groundwater
sources, mostly from shallow aquifers recharged by the Xiaoling and Nuer Rivers. As
with other cities in Liaoning, industry dominates water use, accounting for about
75 percent of consumption, much of which is by independent groundwater abstractions.
Considerable industrial development is proposed, particularly in designated development
zones. Water demand is therefore projected to increase from the present level of
approximately 540,000 n3/d to about 980,000 m3/d in 2010.
39.         Of the seven water sources serving Jinzhou, four are within the city limits
and are at risk of contamination as a result of poor wastewater management, in particular
the discharge of inadequately treated wastewater to the Xiaoling River. The Bozi water
source already needs to be taken out of service due to local contamination of the aquifer
by recharge by untreated wastewater.
40.         Treatment of the wastewater from the city is planned, but there is urgent
need to address projected shortfalls in water supply, which would inhibit industrial growth,
and to safeguard the supply sources. In view of the limited groundwater resources in the
city area and the potential risks of contamination, it would be increasingly necessary to
exploit groundwater resources more distant from the city. A new abstraction from an
aquifer recharged by the Daling River to the east of Jinzhou is proposed.
41.         The Jincheng Paper Mill is located on the Daling River about 25 km north
east of Jinzhou and about 15 kn upstream of the proposed groundwater abstraction for
Jinzhou. The mill is an independent accounting unit subordinated to the Jinzhou Municipal
Light Industry Bureau and has been operating for in excess of 50 years. The annual
turnover of the mill is some Y 320 million.
42.         The mill produces 100,000 tons per year of paper and cardboard, using reed
as the raw material and operating the magnesium sulfite pulping process. The use of water
and raw materials at the mill is inefficient, and the mill discharges a highly polluting
wastewater stream. Water consumption is some 220 m3 per ton of paper produced, which
is several times that at efficient pulp and paper mills elsewhere. In addition, the pollution
load of the wastewater from the mill is some 3 million population equivalent, and the
discharge is the most significant single discharge to the Daling River. There is very
limited dilution in the Daling River, and the discharge from the mill severely degrades
water quality in the river. As a result, there is concern regarding the potential effect of
the discharge on water quality in the aquifer from which future abstractions for Jinzhou are
proposed.
Environmenlul Rehabilitation and Construction
43.         Many suitable heritage conservation activities were reviewed in the
preparation of the project. The four activities presented below were chosen as they form
a cohesive package that covers a broad range of heritage issues and are spread
geographically OLroughout the province. Activities at the Shenyang Palace Museum and
the medieval walled city of Xing Cheng, judged also to be priorities, await funding.



- 64 -                        ANNEX2
44.         Museum Services. Although the Liaoning Provincial Museum in Shenyang
is acknowledged by museum experts as one of the world's greatest collections of Chinese
art, particularly its early paintings and textiles, it is relatively unknown and underutilized
in Shenyang and the province. Under this activity, parts of the Liaoning Provincial
Museum would be renovated in order to improve the environmental and security conditions
in galleries and storerooms and to rationalize the use of the existing buildings. A
Conservation Center would be installed and equipped, the only such teaching facility in the
Province. An exhibition introducing the history and material culture of Liaoning and
related publicity material would be prepared to increase the museum's visibility and
popularity in Shenyang. The museum is slated to become the focus of training activities
for the project, incorporating training in preventive conser  on methods, presentation and
interpretation of material culture, programming and budg   g, and management.
45.         Niuhellang. One of the most important archaeological sites in Liaoning, and
perhaps China, Niuheliang is a key to understanding the early history of the Hongshan
culture (circa 3000 BC) and Liu people. Archaeological work is under way, but adequate
site conservation is lacking. Planning for the development of the site for visitors has not
been carried out. The site is in urgent need of emergency conservation of areas in which
archaeological excavation has commenced and which are now at serious risk from the
effects of the extreme climate and lack of security. In addition, site management works,
supported by a conceptual plan for its long-term development, would meet archaeological
investigation and site and conservation needs, its presentation for visitors, and future
infrastructure requirements.
46.         Jiumenkou Great Wall. The Great Wall at Jiumenkou, a World Heritage
Site, consists of expanses of wall, observation and signal towers, and a nine-arched bridge
across the Jiujiang River. This strategic pass of the Great Wall is located in a dramatic
landscape some 30 minutes from the established tourist resorts of Shanhaiguan and the
Bohai Sea. Although parts of the WaU at Jiumenkou have been repaired, the remaining
unprotected areas are threatened by decay that is hastened by uncontrolled visitor access.
At present, there are no educational or tourism promotion functions at the site.
47.         Conservation of the Wall is needed that would develop and apply
nonintrusive, scientifically sound methods to stabilize a section of the Wall. In tandem
with appropriate visitor management, the negative impacts from visitors would be reduced.
Conservation techniques used here would be applicable at other sites in Liaoning. Site
presentation would focus on improving existing visitor facilities, increasing the visitors'
understanding and enjoyment of the Great Wall, and improving revenue earnings. The
geographical position and ecological setting of Jiumenkou, the historical background and
construction history of the Wall, and great personalities associated with Jiumenkou would
be presented to visitors. Additional funding is being sought for the preparation and
implementation of a comprehensive Master Plan at Jiumenkou that would assess the
significance of the site, its infrastructure requirements, sociocultural setting, financing
options, and tourism potential. This would be an instrument to reconcile the historical and
conservation sensitivity of the site with its development as a major historic place.



- 65 -                         ANNEX 2
48.         Jieshi Gong. The archaeologial site of Jieshi Gong, covering an area of
over 25 kn2, contains traces of early settlements and has been identified with the palace
of the third century BC Emperor Qinshihuan. The investigation of the site is at an early
stage; interest in the area for tourism development is growing. A macro-site investigation
of the site, including systematic archaeological survey, is required to form a sound basis
for any future development. Selected site conservation works in excavated areas would
also be undertaken.
49.         Sectoral Issues. Traditionally, cultural heritage conservation has existed at
the margins, carried out with little coordination with line agencies and small budgets.
Awareness of environmental issues has provided an opportunity to bring heritage
conservation into the national development agenda. Several issues, however, remain to be
adequately considered. Development control and financing are central to the discussion.
A critical issue, which would need concerted efforts, is how development control
mechanisms can be used to protect the heritage. At present in Liaoning Province, and in
China in general, heritage is considered primarily as individual monuments. Little
attention is paid to the physical setting and context of historic structures. The result is that
the historic fabric is rapidly being lost, as in the case of the surroundings of the Imperial
Palace in Shenyang where a large, multistory shopping complex has been constructed
adjacent to the Palace, severely compromising its integrity. As land markets become more
active and property prices in the centers of historic cities increase, the threat to heritage
would also intensify. The use of financial instruments to fund heritage activities is largely
unexplored. At this critical juncture in the move to a market-based economy, heritage
would need to maximize its ability to generate revenue in order to survive. Experience has
shown that wouldingness to pay depends largely on offering the public archaeological and
historical sites and museums with quality presentation and interpretation. It can be
expected that in China, as discretionary income and leisure time increase, visitorship to
cultural sites would also rise.

DETAIED PROJECT DESC ON
1.          The components of the proposed project investments arc described in detail
below and summarized in tabular form.
Anshan Wastewater Colection, Treatment and Disposal
2.          The Anshan wastewater component is designed to address the pollution of
the Xida Gou canal, which is a tributary of the Yunliang River and ultimately the Taizi.
The Xida Gou carries mainly industrial wastewater (about 93 percent), principally from
the ANGANG steel complex in Anshan. The component would also provide opportunities
for reuse of the treated wastewater for a number of purposes in the ANGANG plant for
water conservation objectives.  The principal wastewater characteristics are high
concentrations of suspended solids and oil.
3.          The present dry weather flow is estimated around 192,000 m3/day, based on
field survey data obtained in 1992. Significant increases in wastewater flows are not
expected since restricted water availability is already a limiting factor for industry in
Anshan. Future industrial development would be forced to incorporate better water
management and reuse policies that would offset any total increase in water demand.
4.          The design average flow for the facility is 220,000 m3l/da with the treatment
process units designed to deal with peak flows of 440,000 m3/day. Treatment of the
wastewater is based on a process sequence comprising grit removal, chemically assisted
primary clarification using alum, and sand filtration. Sludge generated by the treatment
would be dewatered by pressure filtration (filter belt presses) and disposed of at a suitable
landfill. The treated effluent would be mostly reused withir. ANGANG operations and the
treatment process is designed to produce effluent to comply with the ANGANG
specification for recycled water in terms of suspended solids and oil concentrations. The
estimated shown below is a total cost including all contingencies.
5.          Proposed Component ($45.0 million). The proposed component would
comprise the following works:
(a)   Interceptor Culvert of reinforced concrete multiple section construction,
about 4.2 km length, size about 1.5 m wide * 2.0 m high;
(b)   Wastewater Treatment Works to provide effective treatment for about
220,000 m3/d of wastewater for industrial reuse purposes. The process
units would include screening, grit removal, chemically assisted primary



- 67 -                        ANNEX 3
clarification, filtration, sludge dewatering and removal, and onward
pumping of treated wastewater for reuse; and
(c)   Treated Effluent Pumping Main comprising about 3 km of twin steel or
other pipe materials in size about 1,000 mm diameter for industrial reuse.
Fushun Wastewater Collection, Treatment and Disposal
6.          The objective of wastewater collection and treatment in Fushun is to improve
water quality in the Hun River, which is the primary recharge source of the Shenyang
aquifer from which the city of Shenyang derives its potable water supply and the Shenfu
canal, which is extensively used for irrigation. The project concept is based on the
collection of wastewater flows from the city and conveyance by a 26 kn interceptor sewer
for treatment at a new treatment plant at Sanbaotun, downstream of Fushun, and
subsequent discharge to the Hun River. Treatment is by chemically assisted primary
sedimentation, with dewatering and disposal of sludge by landfill. Wastewater flows from
20 major industries in Fushun are to be excluded from the sewerage system and treated on
site for subsequent disposal by discharge to the Shenfu canal. The objective is to improve
water quality in the Hun River downstream of Fushun to Class m and in the Shenfu canal
to Class V.
7.          The average industrial wastewater flow for the design year for the sewerage
system of 2000 is estimated at 230,000 m3/d and average municipal wastewater flow at
some 250,000 m3/d. The interceptor capacity would allow for about 2.4 times the average
flow. The first-phase wastewater treatment plant capacity is 250,000 m3/d, and a pilot
plant facility of capacity 250 m3/d would be provided to determine longer-term design
criteria for secondary treatment.
8.          Proposed Component ($47.8 million). The works to be implemented under
this component are:
(a)   Municipal Interceptor Sewer totaling about 14.5 km of reinforced concrete

Benid Wastewater Collection, Treatment and Disposal
9.          The objective of the Benxi wastewater component is to address the existing
gross pollution of the Taizi River, as it traverses the city, by untreated domestic and
industrial wastewater discharges. This has resulted in deterioration of water quality in the
Taizi downstream of Benxi, where it fails to comply with even the Class V standard, and
in the Shenwo reservoir, which releases water into the Taizi for abstraction for use in
Liaoyang and Anshan.
10.        The main city area would be served by two treatment systems; a municipal
plant at Linjiawaizi on the northem bank of the Taizi would treat an average flow of
300,000 m31d of a mixture of domestic and industrial wastewater in roughly equal
proportions, and a second industrial plant, to be located at Tuanshanzi, on the south bank,
would treat the industrial flows from the main industrial area in the center and southwest
of the city, principaly from the BENGANG steel compleA. In the longer term, a third,
much smaller plant would treat mainly industrial wastewater from the northeastern areas
of the city. Treatment at the municipal wastewater treatment plant would take the form
of grit removal, chemically assisted primary clarification and sludge dewaterng by filter-
belt pressing for disposal at a suitable nearby landfill facility.
11.        Wastewater would be conveyed to the proposed municipal wastewater
treatment plant by a large interceptor sewer, some 12 km in length, traversing the majority
of the city and running adjacent to the river. This sewer would intercept dry-weather
flows of municipal and industrial wastewater currently discharging to the Taizi via outfalls
and open channels. The wastewater to be treated at the industrial wastewater treatment
plant would conveyed to the plant by a separate interceptor adjacent to the river on the
south bank.
12.        The objective of treatment is to improve the water quality in the Taizi River
to Class IV. Collection and treatment of the combined municipal wastewater would make
a significant improvement in water quality in the Taizi, but would not be sufficient to
achieve atl water quality objectives.  It is important, therefore, that the industrial
wastewater collection and treatment facilities be provided as a matter of urgency.
13.        Proposed Component ($39.9 million). The proposed project component
would comprise the following works for the municipal system (the industrial system would
be implemented under a separate program):
(a)   Municipal Interceptor Sewer totaling about 5.5 km of reinforced concrete
or other pipe materials in sizes from about 900 mm to 1,600 mm diameter;



- 69 -                        AEX 3
(b)   Municipal Interceptor Sewer totaling about 6.6 km of reinforced concrete
single-section closed culvert in sizes from about 2.3 m wide by 1.8 m high
to 2.8 m wide by 1.9 m high;
(c)   Link Sewers and Connections totaling about 7 km of reinforced concrete
or other pipe materials in sizes from about 300 mm to 800 mm diameter;
and
(d)   Wastewater Treatment Plant at Linjiawaizi to provide treatment for about
300,000 m3ld of mixed domestic and industrial wastewater. The process
would include screening, grit removal, chemically assisted primary
clarfication, and sludge dewatering/landfill disposal.
Benxi Air Qualky Management and Dtrict Heating
14.        Benxi has a Master Plan for Air Pollution Abatement (APA), prepared by
the Benxi EPB, which is in two phases, covering the periods 1991-96 and 1996-2000. The
objective to be achieved by 1996 is to comply with Class mI of the national air quality
standards (the least stringent) on an annual average basis for aU pollutants except for SO2
for which the goal is an annual average of 0.15 mglm3 (1.5 times the Class Im standard).
The 2000 objective is compliance with the Class II standard for all pollutants other than
S02 for which the objective is the Class m standard.
15.        The first stage of the APA program is therefore aimed primarily at removal
of TSP, with a lower priority attached to SO2. Proposals are in hand for substantial
reductions in TSP from the Benxi power station, operated by BENGANG, by means of the
installation of electrostatic precipitators on the boiler plant.  BENGANG would
neverteless remain the most significant source of TSP and S02 emissions in Benxi.
16.        This component would comprise pollution control investments at the
BENGANG blast furnaces and coke ovens and provision of boilers, heat exchangers and
distribution pipework forming part of an expansion of the Benxi district heating system.
The subcomponents are as follows.
17.        Blast Furnaces. The existing wet scrubbers for solids removal from the off-
gases at two blast fumaces would be replaced by dry electrostatic precipitators. This
would have the effect of obviating a polluting wastewater discharge to the Taizi and of a
small reduction in the discharge of TSP to atmosphere. The primary benefit, however, is
that the off-gases would be of suitable quality for use in the BENGANG coke ovens and
hence the recovery of their energy content.
18.        This reuse would have the beneficial effect of allowing the coke oven gas
currently used to be diverted for beneficial use in the town gas supply network for
residential areas, and would approximately double the availability of town gas. This in
turn would reduce the consumption of coal in small inefficient domestic boilers and stoves,
which is particularly important in terms of its effect on the ground-level concentrations of



- 70 -                        ANNEX 3
the important pollutants S02 and TSP. To realize the maximum environmental and
economic benefits of the proposa!, it is imperative that the town gas distribution system in
Benxi is of sufficient capacity to utilize fully the additional supplies and must be expanded
if necessary.
19.        Coke Ovens. The objective of this subcomponent is to reduce the emissions
of particulates that occur during the charging of the coke ovens with coal and the coke side
pushing and quenching. Although not highly significant in terms simply of TSP, the
emissions are all fugitive and at or near ground level and therefore are very significant in
terms of local ground-level pollutant concentrations. Of particular concern are the
emissions from the coal-charging operations, which contain the carcinogenic benzol-soluble
organics (BSO) and benzopyrene (Bap), which are associated with the emissions of TSP
and condense and adhere to the particulate matter and are easily inhaled. They are
therefore of considerable concem in terms of both occupational and environmental health.
20.        The process selected for the control of emissions from charging of the coke
ovens is based on a new automatic charging car design with adjustments to the sequencing
and rate of coal charging. The pushing emission control proposal is based on the
*Minister-Stein" process and retrofitting of the existing quench towers, which collectively
reduce emissions from both the pushing of the coke into the quench car and during the
quench cycle.
21.        District Heating. This subcomponent provides distribution pipework, heat
exchangers and supplementary boilers to support a district heating scheme to increase the
residential heating supply area in Benxi by approximately 1 million m2 based primarily on
a 28 MW CHP plant. The overall scheme would have a substantial environmental benefit
in reducing the consumption of coal in small inefficient boilers in residential areas and
hence the emissions of SO2 and TSP to atmosphere. There would be a furnher reduction
in emissions of both TSP and SO2 because emission controls would be incorporated in the
CHP boiler plant, in the case of SO2 removal in the form of in-fumace injection of
limestone.
22.         As a result of imbalances in demands for heat and electricity in terms of the
CHP plant output in winter, it is necessary to provide supplementary heat-only boilers for
winter operation. In order to sustain the environmental benefits of the scheme as a whole,
these boilers would also be equipped with emission controls.
23.         The cumulative effect of these proposals is to reduce emissions of TSP by
about 20 percent and S02 by about 7 percent in terms of annual emissions. Although two
of the three subcomponents are directed at BENGANG, the overall mass emissions from
BENGANG are not greatly reduced in terms of the principal pollutants at which the Benxi
APA Master Plan is directed, namely TSP and 52. The reductions in emissions derive
principally from the reductions in the use of coal in residential areas by means of improved
energy efficiency and fuel substitution, in part brought about by the BENGANG blast
furnace subcomponent. Thus, the component is consistent both with the objectives of the



- 71-                         ANNEX3
Benxi APA Master Plan and with the Bank's ongoing policy dialogue with China in the
context of a change from coal to cleaner fuels for urban energy supply.
24.         In practice, the effect of the component in terms of improvements in air
quality in Benxi is expected to be greater than implied simply by the annual reductions in
emissions for two reasons:
(a)  the reductions in emissions derived from fuel substitution are achieved by
replacing small boilers in residential areas that have no emission controls,
are inefficient and discharge emissions close to ground level; their effect on
ground-level concentrations of pollutants is therefore disproportionate to the
mass of pollutant emitted; and
(b)   part of the reduction is seasonal, occurring only over the five-month heating
period; thus the emission reduction on a daily basis is proportionally
increased.
25.         Proposed Component ($72.2 million). This component would comprise:
Air Quality Management
(a)   BENGANG Coke Ovens Nos. 1, 2 and 4, comprising the supply and
installation of automatic charging car facilities with emission controls and
retrofitting of existing quench towers; and
(b)   BENGANG Blast Furnaces Nos. 1 and 2, comprising the provision of dry
electrostatic precipitators for control of polluting wastewater discharges to
Taizi River and energy recovery from utilization of off-gases.
