PROJECT JUSTMCATION AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. PROJECr JUSTICATION
1.         Liaoning Province has developed a provincial investment plan designed to
ameliorate major environmental problems and provide the basis for sustainable
development. The development of that plan was assisted in part by a 1986 study conducted
jointly with AIDAB, the UNDP, and the World Bank, which identified inadequate water
supply, wastewater management, water and air pollution control, and urban transport as
major impediments to sustainable development in Liaoning Province. Further research
work carried out by LPG, with financial assistance from AIDAB, formulated strategic
plans to ameliorate those problems in cities in the Hun-Taizi river basins, which produce
about 60 percent of Liaoning's industral output, and in other major cities in the province.
Based on Liaoning's prioritized investment plan, the Bank helped finance projects in
Shenyang, Fuxm and Yingkou municipalities under the Liaoning Urban Infrstructure
Project (Credit 2219-CHA). The project now proposed addresses needs in the five cities
of Anshan, Benxi, Dalian, Fushun, and Jinzhou, identified in the investment plan as having
the next highest priority needs.
2.          Investments included in the proposed loan address specific environmental
improvement needs, but more importantly either contribute directly to the restoration of
water quality in the Hun-Taizi river basin, support exemplary policy initiatives, or support
solutions new to China. The Hun-Taizi cleanup effort depends critically on reducing
poilution loads from the major industral cities of Fushun, Benxi, and Anshan, all of which
would build wastewater treatment plants under the loan. Anshan would provide teriary
treatment and resell all treated wastewater. The switch from wet scrubbers to electrostatic
precipitators to clean blast furnace gases in Benxi would eliminate a major source of highly
toxic phenols and cyanide in the Taizi River. Other water pollution control efforts include
that in the Jincheng paper mill, the largest source of pollution in the Daling river basin,
with a 3 million population equivalent. There, process changes to reduce wastewater and
to use the main pollutant-lignosulfonates-to produce a marketable binder provide
pollution reduction at far less cost than conventional treatment alternatives. Dalian would
rely on conventional treatment for its mixed industrial and domestic wastewaters, but add
tertiary treatment that allows sale of treated wastewater for industrial uses in this water-
short city.
3.          Support for water supply capacity expansion in Jinzhou is justified by their
aggressive use of prices to control demand and support their expansion program. Jinzhou
acted without extemal pressure to raise water prices to levels that provide a rate of return
in excess of 20 percent. The 42 percent industrial and 66 percent residential price
incteases have been in effect for only five months, so conclusions about the actual



- 157 -                       ANNEX 14
reduction in excess demand would be premature, but the water company has already noted
a substantial fall in peak summer demand. Dalian, too, has increased tariffs to moderate
demand, an action that would strengthen the market for recycled water to be produced in
the Bank-supported wastewater treatment plants. Dalian's tariffs are -""w among the
highest in China and to take advantage of new industrial interest in saving water, the Bank
loan supports a pilot program of industrial and institutional process change demonstrating
low-cost strategies for water saving.
4.          Benxi has the dubious distinction of having the most intense air pollution of
any city in China, with winter peak SO2 levels well above the level (Class III) at which
China reckons children and other vulnerable people must restrict their activity. The Bank
identified, and would support through the loan, elements of the Benxi long-term air
pollution abatement program designed particularly to reduce ground-level pollution. The
electrostatic precipitators for the blast furnaces mentioned in para. 2 would not only reduce
water pollution, but also provide heat for coke ovens, in turn releasing enough coke oven
gas to double the town gas supply in Benxi and free 50,000 households from reliance on
coal stoves. A retrofit of the coking ovens with a closed charging and pushing system, the
first such retrofit in China, would reduce fugitive emissions of benzopyrene and other
highly carcinogenic organic chemicals by 96 percent, protecting coke oven and nearby
workers from toxic exposure. Investments in the district heating system would allow the
displacement of 26 inefficient boilers that cannot be economically fitted with pollution
control systems and most of the 10,000 individual stoves used for household heat. The
overall effect of these investments would be an estimated 10,200 tons per year reduction
in total Sa loads (7.1 percent of the total) and 45,000 tons per year reduction in
particulates (19.6 percent of the total). The impact would be greater than the proportions
suggest, because all averted emissions are from low stacks, with their highly concentrated
discharges in residential areas.
5.          Despite the rapid degradation of ground, surface, and coastal waters in
Liaoning, not a single landfill in the province operates to sanitary landfill standards. The
solid waste component of the project supports the development of such a landfill in Dalian,
where leachate from a coastal landfill site threatens highly profitable shrimp farms and
other aquaculture. In addition, landfill gasses would be collected through a system
allowing future use of the methane in electricity generation. However, methane output
would not reach levels justifying power generation investments until after the project
period, hence would not be funded by the project. The landfill would serve as a model
for others in the province. To complement those investments, an improved transfer station
would be built in the city and transport upgraded to reduce the number of trips needed to
haul urban refuse to the landfill site. The transfer station and landfill management would
be shifted from the municipal government to a newly formed corporation, which would
allow the efficiency gains possible with increased managerial independence and accrual
accounting.
6.          Perhaps the most innovative program element is that protecting the cultural
heritage of Liaoning, which includes assets of international renown. Termed support for
environmental rehabilitation and reconstruction" in project documents, this component



-158-                         ANNEX 14
recognizes that rapid industrial and commercial development can lead to the equally rapid,
although perhaps inadvertent, destruction of cultural heritage. This comes through high
air and water pollution, which can erode the physical fabric of monuments and relics, and
through the failure to plan or site new construction in a manner sympathetic to existing
historical properties. The component would demonstrate the potential to preserve, develop,
and present cultural properties in a manner that enhances their value and provides benefits
such as increased tourist revenues.
B. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
7.          Project components were selected based on the criteria described in the
above paragraphs. Those criteria set only performance goals-reduction of water and air
pollution, protection from solid waste pollution, increased water supplies, and cultural
preservation-they do not specify the best means of achieving the goal. The specific
means of achieving the goals is unique to the circumstances of each locality and are
described in the following paragraphs. The discussion is grouped undr- the larger
categories of water pollution abatement; water supply; air pollution abatement; solid waste
handling; and cultural preservation. However, it must be recognized that individual project
components can address more than one concern, as do the blast furnace scrubbers in Benxi
or the Anshan and Dalian wastewater treatment plants. For several components, especially
wastewater treatment, benefits are difficult and expensive to quantify and analysis has been
1imited to identifying the least-cost option to meet a performance standard. In others,
benefit streams are more readily quantified and in those cases rates of return have been
estimated.
C. AFORDABIL1TY
8.          Water. The present Jinzhou water supply tariff was enacted before project
appraisal and has been effective for some six months without evidence of difficulty for any
part of the population. Projected increases are within the range of projected real income
increases, thus affordability should not become an issue.
9.          Wastewater. Because the wastewater tariffs proposed for Anshan, Benxi,
Fushun, and Dalian are linked to water consumption, they would substantially increase the
6ost of such consumption. Based on provincial average figures, per capita consumption
of water equaled 158 liters/day in 1992. Assuming that the poorest 5 percent consume
130 liters/day, then no more than an average of about 1.4 percent of their Y 1,005/year
income would be spent on water. Anticipated wastewater charges are well above current
water fees (which are in most cases below the level needed for a positive rate of return on
water company capital), so if those households make no adjustments in their consumption,
an additional 2.2 percent of their income would go to the sewerage tariff. This would
represent, in effect, a significant increase in the cost of water. However, the combined
cost of 3.6 percent of income is not especially high by developing country standards and
at the postulated consumption levels, room exists to reduce consumption, hence payments.



- 159-                         ANNE4
10.         Industry would also face much highier charges for the combined water/
wastewater service.  However, research has shown high levels of waste in current
industrial water use. Industries are expected to quickly adopt some in the range of water-
saving techniques that have been introduced to China in recent years, thereby mitigating
the impact of these charges on production costs.
D. WATER POLIAMION ABtATEMENT
Anshan Wastewater
11.         Background. Anshan, with an urban population of nearly 1. I million, is
home to some 500 major industries, including mining, metallurgical, chemical processing,
petrochemicals, dyeing, and paper making. The city's largest employer, the Anshan Iron
and Steel Corporation, is also China's largest iron and steel producer. Through Anshan
flow the Nanshan, Yunliang, and Yangliu Rivers, tributaries to the Taizi, which passes to
the west of the city. Wastewater flows total about 520,000 ml/day, of which some
48 percent receives treatment at source.  Despite those treatment efforts, Anshan
contributes a substantial pollution load to the Taizi River and that load, combined with
those from Fushun and Benxi, result in Taizi water quality failing to reach Class V
standards. Nonetheless, downstream agricultural communities continue to draw irrigation
water from the river. While those same communities nttempt to avoid the Taizi pollution
by drinking groundwater, the Taizi recharges the aNuifers they draw on, aquifers now
beginning to show measurable pollution levels.
12.         As part of the provincial program to ensure sustainable water supplies for
productive uses, Anshan has been required to protect Taizi river quality by treating
wastewater to the Liaoning integrated wastewater discharge standard II.
13.         Fconomic Analysis. The value of meeting the water quality goal can, at
least theoretically, be evaluated in terms of reduced water supply and treatment costs for
downstream communities, the positive health impacts of avoiding heavy metal uptake by
irrigated crops, increased value of the fish catch, increases in recreational use of
downstream waters, and increases in the amenity value of the Taizi. However, these
benefits are external to Anshan, and the large number of polluters results in a complex
waste flow, frrem which measuring the marginal improvement attributable to Anshan
treatment would be a challenging modeling exercise. The decision for Anshan is therefore
treated as one of identifying the least costly treatment strategy to meet the specified water
quality goals. The economic analysis is therefore based on the least-cost approach.
14.         As noted earlier, three rivers flow through Anshan, and the city has
developed a program that would treat wastewater flows in each basin separately. The
decision to proceed in this way has not been subject to economic analysis, but is consistent
with the flown  in each system and the layout of the existing sewerage system. The project
under disciwsm,on is the first phase of the program, that designed to treat the Yunliang river
basini wastc;ldt flows. Those flows are picked up by two interceptors that converge on
the Xidagoii Canal.  The present dry-weather flow averages about 192,000 m3/day,



-160-                         ANNEX 14
93 percent of which is from industrial sources, primarily Anshan Iron and Steel
Corporation plants. The wastewater is characterized by high suspended solids and oil and
low BOD. Significant increases in flows are not anticipated, since additional water would
be available to local industry only through recycling.
15.        The layouit of the existing interceptors and the constraint on increased
wastewater flows determined both siting and capacity of the treatment works. The works
have been sited at the first flat area downstream of the interceptor intersection that could
be acquired without displacing farm housing. The design flow has been set at 220,000 ml/
day, a 10 percent margin over base flows, with a peak flow capacity of 440,000 m3Jday
needed to cope with storm flows in the combined system.
16.        The solution to the least-cost problem has an additional consideration in
Anshan. Water shortages in the city have created a market for recycled water and
designers were therefore faced with two options:
(a)   The provision of primary treatment, consisting of screening, grit removal,
and chemically assisted clarification; and
(b)   Option (a), plus secondary and tertiary treatment, together with transmission
pipelines for the recycled water.
17.        Because option (a) is the minimum necessary to comply with provincial
discharge guidelines, the choice of option would depend on whether the additional cost of
option (b) would be covered by the expected economic returns from water resales. Table 1
presents the marginal economic returns to option (b). The estimated ERR is 23 percent,
based on the current resale price for treated waste water. The high ERR results from the
fact that the primary treatment facility and operating costs are not attributed to treated
wastewater production. The primary treatment was dictated as a solution to Taizi
pollution, and would have occurred in any case. A second reason for the high return is
that the consumer has agreed to build and maintain the transmission pipelines at their own
expense, reducing both investment and operating costs. Indeed, the estimated ERR is a
lower bound in that it does not incorporate any estimate of the consumer surplus realized
by the Anshan Iron and Steel Company (ANGANG), which has contracted to take the
entire output of treated wastewater.
18.        The ERR supports the Anshan municipal government's selection of option
(b). Were costs to increase a further 20 percent above the physical contingency already
included in the investment costs, and sales reach only half of those projected, the ERR
would still reach 8 percent. Given that the 8 percent underestimates the benefits, for
reasons given in para. 14, the investment appears robust to changes in costs and sales.
19.        Risks. The primary treatment stage of the wastewater treatment plant
presents few risks. The technology is used successfully in other parts of China and
presents few operational challenges. The terdiary treatment for reuse also uses well-known
technology, but success in meeting reuse quality standards would be difficult without



-161 -                         ANNEX 14
adequate control of influent quali'ty. That quality would not now allow the plant to meet
quality standards consistently and thus be able to sell treated wastewater. Influent quality
therefore becomes key to successful plant operation. This problem would be prohibitively
difficult were not the main source of raw wastewater also the sole consumer of the treated
wastewater. ANGANG badly needs the treated wastewater to replace more expensive fresh
water. In testament to that need, ANGANG is building the pipeline from the treatment
plant to its own network. ANGANG workshops contribute 80 percent of the wastewater
stream treated by the plant, with another 8 percent from unproblematic domestic sources.
ANGANG's strong vested interest in assuring that treated wastewater can be supplied,
coupled with their control of the quality of the influent, minimizes the risk of low influent
quality preventing successful treatment for resale. The ANGANG management has agreed
to implement a wastewater quality-based treatment tariff that would protect treatment plant
financial interests when influent quality prevents production of saleable water.
Benxi Wastewater
20.         Background. The Taizi River bisects Benxi City, one of China's largest
steel producers. In addition to steel, Benxi produces an estimated 680,000 ml/day of
industrial, commercial, institutional and residential wastewater. Industries treat some of
their wastewater, although in many cases the resulting flow still fails to meet national
discharge standards. About 10 percent of the total wastewater enters the existing sewerage
system, with the remainder discharged to natural watercourses that drain to the Taizi
River, or to the river itself. The existing sewerage system also discharges to the Taizi
without treatment.
21.         Benxi itself suffers few ill effects from its highly polluting wastewater. The
municipal water company abstracts water upstream of the city, where the Taizi meets
class II quality standards, comfortably above the minimum quality for drinking water
supply. Industries extract within city boundaries, but above their own waste discharge
points. However, at the downstream edge of the city the water no longer meets Class V
standards, the lowest quality deemed usable for any productive purpose. The Taizi then
flows but a few kllometers to the Shenwo Reservoir, the main water source for Liaoyang,
a major industrial city of 660,000 people. The Taizi's self-purification capacity cannot
match the pollution load and fish in the river have been declared inedible due to their high
concentrations of heavy metals.
22.         Benxi Municipality has agreed to bring Taizi water quality up to the
Class IV level below the city-a level that permits irrigation, industrial, and recreational
use. Meeting this goal is expected to raise Shenwo water quality to Class III, the
minimum for a drinking water source.
23.         Economic Analysis. The value of meeting the water quality goal can, at
least theoretically, be evaluated in terms of reduced water treatment costs in downstream
communities, increased value of the fish catch, increases in recreational use of downstream
waters, and nicreases in the amenity value of the Taizi. As a practical matter, sewage
treatment in Benxi is motivated by political pressure to restore Taizi quality to a level that



- 162 -                       ANNEX 1-4
no longer threatens the health of downstream communities and the decision for Benxi
therefore becomes one of identifying the least costly treatment strategy to meet that goal.
The economic analysis is therefore based on the least-cost approach.
24.         Meeting the Taizi water quality goal requires an aggressive program of
wastewater collection and treatment. Economies of scale dictate centralized collection and
treatment of residential, institutional and commercial sewage, individual sources of which
now discharge to a small number of streams tributary to the Taizi. However, industrial
wastewater may be produced in sufficient quantity and have specific enough treatment
needs to warrant treatment at source, followed by discharge to the sewerage system or to
the Taizi. Additionally, the geographic coverage*  'le reticulation system needs to be
optimized. Initial work on these problems producet  z following four options:
(a)   Complete coverage by the reticulation system, with a treatment plant
capacity of 700,000 m3/day and primary treatment (screening, grit removal,
and chemically assisted primary clarification).
(b)   Exclusion of industrial enterprises that meet discharge standards, with plant
capacity to be a function of industrial commitments to meet those standards.
(c)   Exclusion of the northeast corner of the city, with a 32,000 m3/day sewage
flow, from the reticulation system.
(d)   The combination of options 2 ano 3, implying a treatment plant size of
about 300,000 m3/day.
25.         The fourth option was chosen, for reasons described briefly below and in
more detail in the feasibility study.
26.         The Benxi Iron and Steel Company (BENGANG) produces far more
industrial wastewater than any other source in Benxi.  Furthermore, the chemical
composition of many BENGANG wastewaters forces pretreatment before they can enter
a municipal system. BENGANG was offered the option of treating wastewater to the
national standard before direct discharge or, after necessary pretreatment, discharging to
the municipal system for further treatment. After reviewing the cost of each option,
BENGANG elected self-treatment and wastewater would therefore not enter the municipal
system. This allows a concomitant reduction in interceptor and treatment plant capacity.
27.         BENGANG developed a plan that would, by the end of 1997, ensure that
all wastewater meets national standards. Crucial elements of the plan include switching
from wet scrubbers to dry scrubbers in the blast furnaces and power plant (another
component of this loan finances blast furnace scrubbers, which for the Xihu number 1 and
2 fumaces would reduce wastewater by 39,000 m3/day); expanding treatment units for the
coldng ovens (work on which would be finished by the end of 1993); and increasing
wastewater treatment and reuse in other parts of the mill. Full application of the pollution
levy fee, which for BENGANG has risen from Y 10 million in 1991 to Y 17 million in



- 163 -                       ANNEX 14
1993 as fee coverage has expanded, encourages BENGANG to implement fully the
program, which the municipal EPB monitors. As an additional precautionary measure, the
city has set aside a parcel of land near BENGANG for construction of an industrial
wastewater treatment plant should BENGANG fail to meet its goals, or should further
increases in national discharge standards require a higher level of treatment.
28.         BENGANG's choice to opt out of the municipal system plays an important
role in the viability of option 3. A PENGANG plant dominates the northeast corner of
Benxi and produces the bulk of the current sewage flow, all of which would be treated
under BENGANG's plan. The remainiug municipal wastewater would not have sufficient
impact on river quality to justify building the needed interceptor in that area.
29.         The remaining flows from all sources would enter the municipal system,
with industrial flows pretreated as necessary. The current use of stream beds to carry the
bulk of sewage flows allows installation of sanitary sewers adjoining or under the stream
bed. The streams would continue to drain storm waters, and this use of separate drainage
and sewerage systems allows smaller interceptors and a smaller treatment plant to be built.
30.         After the determination of system coverage, hence plant capacity and the
nature of incoming sewage, the next step was to determine the degree of treatment. Unlike
Dalian and Anshan, two other cities with wastewater teatment plants financed by the
project, Benxi does not suffer water shortages for municipal use, while industrial shortages
occur in some years for brief periods in the winter. Therefore, Benxi has no incentive to
move to teriary treatment for water recycling. Effluent tests determined that primary
treatment would remove the heavy metals so inimical to health, as well as reduce other
pollutant loads sufficiently to allow the desired Class IV standard to be met downstream
of the treatment plant. However, the area being acquired for the treatment plant allows
for expansion to secondary treatment if future quality needs dictate.
31.         With the above parameters set, further design alternatives were evaluated
on the basis of financial costs. Because the alternative technologies considered did not
involve radically different proportions of capital and labor, or inputs that vary in
substantially different proportions from world market prices, the financial costs reflect
relative economic costs and determine the least-cost economic solution. Discussion of the
design alternatives can be found in the consultant's report, which is on file.
32.         Risks. The proposed Benxi technology is well-proven and presents few
operating challenges. The major risk in the Benxi effort is that BENGANG not meet its
long-term treatment goals. Measures to minimize that risk were detailed in para. 23,
above. Further, it is worth noting that the Benxi plan is not prospective, but in the mid-
point of implementation. The additional coke oven water treatment facility would be
commissioned by end-1993, and the dry scrubbers on the first two blast furmaces would
be financed under this project. Nonetheless, system performance would be carefully
monitored.



-164-                         ANNEX 14
Dalian Wastewater
33.         Background.  Dalian, with a nonagricultural population of about 2.3
million, is at the southern tip of the Liaodong peninsula, which separates the Bohai Sea
from the Yellow Sea. The city has a diverse economic base that includes one of northeast
China's most important deep-water ports, fisheries, tourism, and industries ranging from
metallurgy to textiles, electronics, food processing and building materials.  Only
20,000 m3/day of a total wastewater stream estimated at 360,0) m3/day is currently
treated before discharge to coastal waters. Population and economic growth have outrun
industrial water-saving measures, resulting in rapid growth of the wastewater stream. The
predominance of industrial wastewater can be seen in the low BOD and high suspended
solids and phenols of the discharges.
34.         The inshore areas of the Dalian, West, and Heishijiao Bays, all important
for tourism, fisheries, aquaculture, and seaweed cultivation, have growing pollutant
concentrations that threaten the viability of important sectors of Dalian's economy. One
wastewater treatment plant, commissioned in 1988, provides the only municipal sewage
treatment. That plant, at Chunliu, serves discharges to the main deep-water port zone but
operates at only one third of its rated 60,000 m3/day capacity due to a lack of link and
interceptor sewers. Dalian has planned a total of eight wastewater treatment plants that
would provide complete coverage of estimated flows by the year 2020. The geography of
Dalian, which is built over a range of hills that create distinct drainage basins, drove the
decision about plant numbers and service areas. Of the seven remaining to be constructed,
the highest priority site is on the Malan River, now an open sewer discharging to Heshijiao
Bay, an important aquaculture and tourist resort area. The Malan also carries the largest
pollution load and wastewater flow of the seven zones.
35.         Economic Analysis: (a) Chunliu Wastewater Treatment Plant. The
existing wastewater treatment facility at Chunliu processes only one third of its design
capacity of 60,000 m3/day. Furthermore, the digesters and sludge dewatering facilities
have never functioned, forcing the plant to discharge sludge to the bay. Although it does
treat a modest amount of wastewater for sale-about 5,000 m3/day-the plant far from
realizes its potential contribution to reducing water pollution in Dalian's coastal waters or
supplying treated wastewater for industrial use.
36.         Chunliu has the advantage of substantial sunk costs needing a supply
constraint relieved to realize ful production. This project would relieve that constraint,
which would have the secondary benefit of providing additional treated wastewater for sale.
Those two factors lead to an incremental economic cost of treating wastewater of only
Y 0.57 per ton (see Table 2), as compared to Y 0.62 per m3 for the next least costly option
in the Dalian wastewater treatment plan. The ultimate purpose of sewage treatment is to
reduce pollution damage and, in this case, relatively low net treatment costs reinforce a
decision driven by the municipal government's ranking of damage from the various
catchments. Chunliu serves a high-density residential and industrial neighborhood
discharging sewage into the central harbor. The need to protect this vulnerable resource
drives the decision to invest in sewers in the Chunliu catchment.



- 165 -                       ANNEX 14
37.         Risks:  (a) Chumllu Wastewater Treatment Plant. The design and
equipment quality failures that kept the original digesters and sludge dewatenng equipment
from being operated would be avoided through more careful engineering and the use of
competitive bidding with strict performance criteria in the current project. That part of the
facility that now operates turns out a high-quality effluent and sells treated wastewater,
encouraging signs for the operation of the expanded facility. Chunliu costs are held down
by the sale of water and any difficulty in marketing the planned output at planned prices
would impair the financial performance of the system. However, the design assumes sales
of only half of the incremental wastewater intake, and that at a price only 40 percent of
the sale price for fresh water. Dalian's freshwater shortages and stringent quotas under
which industries operate encourage use of treated wastewater, so the market risk is
believed slight. With no additional water sales, treatment costs would rise to Y 0.70/mn3
and necessitate a tariff increase.
38.         Economic Analysis: (b) Malan Wastewater Treatment Plant. The Malan
River has a drainage area of 95 kin, capturing the major wastewater flows in southwest
Dalian. In addition to domestic wastewater, the river collects discharges of up to
50,000 m3/day from more than 100 industrial plants. Discharge from the river pollutes the
shoreline near the river mouth and the fishery waters offshore. As the Malan drains into
the most popular tourist beach area in Dalian, and an area of rich offshore fisheries,
including cultured shellfish, control of this effluent stream has also been given priority by
the government.
39.         Two basic design altematives were considered for Malan river treatment.
Both depend on link and interceptor sewers that would separate storm water from sewage
flows, allowing the river to drain the former. The first alternative would provide primary
treatment prior to discharge through a 6 km long marine outfall. The second would
provide secondary treatment, with discharge to the river, and, as demand permitted,
terty treatment for up to half of the wastewater for industrial reuse. Costs were
estimated for both alternatives and the second proved both substantially cheaper and less
risky. Details can be found in the consultant's reports.
40.         The proposed Malan Wastewater Treatment P'ant was the second least costly
to the Chunliu sewerage network extension. Malan also shares with Chunliu the possibility
of selling treated wastewater for industrial use. The net economic costs of treating the
wastewater are approximately Y 0.62/m3 (see Table 3). If no treated wastewater sales
were realized, that cost would increase to Y 0.71/m3.
41.         Risks: (b) Malan Wastewater Treatment Plant. As with the other
wastewater treatment plants, Malan depends on a tested design that would require operating
skills similar to those demonstrated in the Chunliu plant. The fact that Dalian has
operators of proven competence minimizes operational risk. As with Chunliu, the sale of
treated wastewater for industrial use reduces net treatment costs, but a 50 percent decline
in treated wastewater sales would increase net treatment costs by only 6 percent, while a
50 percent increase in sales would reduce costs by 7 percent. Any substantial variation in



- 166 -                       ANNEX  14
resale performnance would be matched by a tariff adjustment to maintain treatment plant
financial performance at the planned level.
Msishun Wastewater
42.         Bacdground. Fushun is a major industrial city with a 1992 population of
about 1.2 million, located on the Hun River about 50 km upstream from Shenyang, the
provincial capital. At present, some industrial wastewaters receive pretreatment and they,
along with untreated domestic, institutional, and other industrial wastewaters, are then
discharged into the Hun River or to the Shenfu Irrigation Canal. The Shenfu Canal itself
discharges to the Hun River, which eventually merges with the Taizi River south of
Shenyang.
43.         In large part because of the 630,000 n/day Fushun wastewater discharges,
neither the Hun River nor the Shenfu Canal meet Category V surface water quality
standards, the lowest standard allowing water use. Despite that, the Shenfu remains the
most important source of irrigation water for farmers to the west of Fushun and the Hun
River recharges the Shenyang aquifer, which begins some 20 km downstream from
Fushun. Pollution of the aquifer has forced Shenyang to meet part of its water needs
through construction of a conveyor to the Dahuofan6, Reservoir, on the Hun River
upstream of Fushun.
44.         As part of the provincial program to ensure sustainable water supplies for
productive uses, Fushun has now been required to protect the Shenyang aquifer by
ensuring that as it approaches the recharge area, the water quality of the Hun reaches
Cls  m, the minimum standard for drinldng water, and that the water quality in the
Shenfu Canal be improved to Class V, the minimum for irrigation use.
45.         Economic Analysis. The value of meeting the water quality goal can, at
least theoretically, be evaluated in terms of reduced water supply and treatment costs for
Shenyang, the positive health impacts of avoiding heavy metal uptake by irrigated crops,
increased value of the fish catch, increases in recreational use of downstream waters, and
increases in the amenity value of the Hun. However, these benefits are external to Fushun
(with the possible exception of health impacts from crops) and the decision for Fushun
therefore becomes one of identifying the least costly treatment strategy to meet the
specified water quality goals. The economic analysis is therefore based on the least-cost
approach.
46.         Approximately 63 percent of the Fushun population already connects to a
sewerage system, which discharges primarily to the Shenfu Canal. Of those discharges,
some 30 percent are of domestic origin and the remainder industdal. The coverage of the
current sewerage system and siting of local pump stations influenced the development of
options to meet the quality goals laid out above. A crucial finding in the feasibility studies
was that treatment of the effluent in the existing sewerage system would allow quality goals
to be met. The third of the flow not presently captured enters the Hun River on the
upstream side of the city, and, with other wastewaters suffici-ntly treated, the Hun has



- 167 -                        ANNEX 14
sufficient self-cleaning capacity to meet the Class III standard at the aquifer without further
treatment of the uncaptured flows.
47.         Analysis of wastewater flows showed that the effluent from 20 large
industrial plants currently treating sewage to national standards and discharging to the
Shenfu Canal could be excluded from the treatment stream while maintaining the Shenfu
Canal water at Class V. Those plants discharge an average of 240,000 ml/day, or about
28 percent of all Fushun flows, allowing a substantial reduction in the design capacity for
the treatment plant. The remaining wastewater can be brought to acceptable quality
through primary treatment consisting of screening, grit removal, and chemically assisted
primary clarification.
48.         Based on those findings and the layout of the existing reticulation system,
two alternative concepts for wastewater collection, treatment and disposal were considered:
(a)   construction of four separate collection/treatment systems to serve Fushun
city districts; and
(b)   collection of all flows, with treatment at a single plant at Sanbaotun to the
south of Fushun.
49.         Both alternatives examined a number of conveyor and plant site/size options,
full description of which can be found in the consultant's feasibility studies. Economies
of scale in treatment outweighed the additional conveyor costs of the single plant option
(option B), which was therefore adopted in this project.
50.         The treatment plant, with a capacity of 250,000 m3/day, has been sized to
meet year 2000 flows. The plant would be located well beyond the current industrial and
residential districts to allow for future urban growth and minimize resettlement. As with
the Anshan and Benxi plants, the financial cost minimizing plant engineering solution to
meet the 250,000 m3/day capacity demand also results in the economic least-cost plant
configuration. Details may be found in the consultant's reports.
51.         In determining which industrial plants would be incorporated into the new
system, Fushun officials surveyed the enterprises not yet meeting national discharge
standards. They established that the cost of in-plant treatment in these enterprises would
exceed the cost of centralized treatment and that the designed mix of in-plant and
centralized treatment is therefore also least cost from the industry's perspective.
52.         The Fushun treatment plant would incorporate a small unit to test strategies
for secondary treatment. This work is being done in anticipation of future further
tightening of ambient quaLty standards and would facilitate least-cost responses to those
future demands. The 110,000 ml/day of wastewater not entering the Shenfu Canal would
continue to discharge to the Hun River near the top of the interceptor through three natral
streams. Those streams serve as storm drains with high wet-season flows, with other flows
primarily from residential and mining areas. The area served is growing very slowly and



-168-                         ANNEX 14
the contribution to Hun River pollution small. For this reason, planning has yet to begin
for investment in an interceptor system and treatment plant to serve this area. However,
Fushun continues to monitor those flows and would take action if they begin to contribute
substantial pollution to the Hun.
53.         Risks. As with the other plants, the proposed Fushun technology is well-
proven and presents few operating challenges. Fushun has already constructed a large
conveyor for their water supply, demonstrating the ability satisfactorily to build and
operate that aspect of the investment. The risks to the system come not in the system
itself, but in the decision to leave major industrial dischargers outside of the system.
Continued, careful monitoring would be necessary to assure that those industries maintain
the quality of their effluent. The environmental protection bureau has responsibility for
this activity and has demonstrated capacity to undertake it.
Jincheng Paper NMiI-Wastewater Reduction and Water Savings
54.         Background. The Jincheng Paper Mill is located some 25 kxn northeast of
Jinzhou on the east bank of the Daling River, about 15 km upstream of a proposed
weilfield for Jinzhou water supply. The mill currently discharges all wastewater without
treatment, delivering a pollution load of some 3 million population equivalent, by far the
largest source of pollution in the Daling river basin. Discharge from the mill increases the
COD concentration in the Daling by up to 200 mg/I during the winter low flows. The
damage inherent in this discharge becomes apparent when contrasted with the allowable
maximum ambient COD concentration of 25 mg/l for the lowest usable surface water. The
Jincheng discharges prevent use of Daling water for irrigation, industrial, or drinking water
downstream of the plant and threaten aquifers through recharge with polluted waters.
55.         Economic Analysis. Mill managers rejected end-of-pipe treatment as
prohibitively expensive and instead invited design institutes to help them identify process
changes that could reduce wastewater discharge. Proposed changes involve two major
components. The first, for white-water recovery, reduces water consumption by some
22,000 m3/day, and allows the recovery of 8,000 tons per year of talc and 4,600 tons per
year of pulp, all of which were previously discharged to the Daling. The second, for red
liquor extraction and evaporation to produce lignosulfonate binder, reduces water
consumption by an additional 18,000 m3/day and permits binder production to increase
from the current 50,000 tons per year to 150,000 tons per year. The implementation of
these two programs would reduce pollution from 3 million to 1 million population
equivalent. The ERR from the white-water recovery is estimated at 37 percent and that
for red liquor extraction and binder production at 9 percent.
56.         A full discussion of the possible process changes for water saving can be
found in the consultant's reports in the Project File (Annex 16 . The white-water recovery
process recycles water used in the pulp-washing operation. It was chosen because it drew
on a known technology that offered substantial water savings as well as recovery of talc
and pulp. The high ERR is driven by the water savings and pulp recovery, where the
water is valued at marginal economic extraction costs as defined by costs in the new



- 169 -                       ANNEX 14
Jinzhou welfield using the same aquifer and pulp at the market price for pulp. It could,
but does not, include avoided damage to the Daling by controlling this important source
of pollution, so can be taken as a lower bound of the true ERR. The ERR would fall to
12 peicent if costs increased 40 percent and benefits fell by the same amount. The
analysis is presented in Table 4.
57.         A full discussion of the investment to increase lignosulfonate binder
production can be found in the consultant's reports in the Project File. The raw material
for the binder is the single most important pollutant from the paper mill. Process
improvements allowed additional water savings, but no reduction in the pollutant itself.
Alternative abatement strategies were to either treat the effluent in a wastewater treatment
plant or to use it to produce more binder. The technology currently used at the plant could
economically convert only the 50,000 tons per year of binder now sold. Energy costs for
additional production have been prohibitive. Ihe technology proposed would allow
production of 150,000 tons per year and a concomitant reduction in lignosulfonate
discharge to the Daling. The ERR of 9 percent (see Table 5) is derived from binder sales,
water savings, and the avoided costs of treating the waste stream. The latter calculation
assumes that the plant would otherwise carry out the EPB mandate to treat water to the
national discharge standard by building a treatment plant. The cost of treating the
wastewater reflect the real resource cost of the least-cost alternative in ameliorating current
damages. No attempt has been made to measure the damages themselves, which would
be a complex and costly exercise.
58.         The results are sensitive to assumptions about sales of binder.  The
calculation assumes that half of the additional output would be produced and sold in the
first year of production, increasing to 75 percent in the second year and full sales by 1998.
However, binder would be produced only to meet market demand, and, if binder is not
produced, the raw material would, following current practice, be discharged untreated to
the Daling River. If incremental sales reach only 68,000 tons, instead of the planned
100,000, the ERR falls to 0. Below that sales point, the treatment plant would have been
the preferred alternative.
59.         Risks. The white-water recovery process faces almost no risks. The
technology has been proven in similar production operations and the recovered water, talc,
and pulp would all be used in the plant itself and therefore do not depend on outside
markets. Conversion would require very little time and the operations and maintenance
are within the technical competence of the current plant staff.
60.         The binder production faces greater risks. Although all inputs are now
produced within the plant, the processing equipment would likely be imported and require
more sophisticated maintenance and operation than the current facility. More important
is the commercial risk already noted. Currently, almost all production goes to a nearby
zinc smelter that uses the product in smelting operations. The smelter is expanding its
production capacity using the current technology and has signed a letter of intent to
purchase an additional 50,000 tons per year of binder. In addition, two factories producing
fire-retardant materials for industrial use have signed letters of intent to purchase the



- 170 -                       ANNEX 14
remaining 50,000 tons per year. Due to high transport costs faced by all producers and
lincheng's close proximity to the smelter, Jincheng has a competitive advantage in
supplying the zinc smelter, which currently uses between 80,000 and 90,000 tons per year
of binder. Jincheng sales staff have detailed information on the seven other binder
producers in China and the only other planning expansion is doing so under a joint venture
with an Indian firm, producing a powdered version not competitive in smelting operations.
The current letters of intent and Jincheng's knowledge of the market minimize the risk of
not reaching the break-even sales point.
E. WATER SUPPLY
Jinzhou Water Supply
61.         Background. Jinzhou, a city with an urban population of some 930,000
people, meets all of its water needs through abstraction from local aquifers. In recent
years, water demand has been constrained through physical quotas as growth of industrial
and residential demand has outstripped supply. That supply has recently become more
precarious as infiltration of industrial pollutants via polluted recharge from the Nuer River
has forced the city to shut down some of the existing wells. In response to the growing
demand gap, Jinzhou has moved aggressively in the past year to control demand through
prices rather than physical quotas and to use the increased income to fund new sources of
supply.
62.         Jinzhou supply choices are further extraction from the Daling and Xiaoling
alluvial fans or the construction of a reservoir on the Daling. The city commissioned a
feasibility study on the latter option three years ago and found that incremental average
supply costs from that source would be approximately twice those from groundwater.
However, the city judges that the 100,000 m3/day supply available under the proposed
project is the last major field they can exploit in aquifers available to them and that fiuther
increases would have to come from either smaller fields or a reservoir, both of which
would be more costly.
63.         Hydrogeologists sited the proposed wellfield after considering the location
of existing wells and the recharge potential of various options. The placement and depth
of individual wells was dictated by the characteristics of the water-bearing strata and
recharge pattems. Alternative placements had virtually no affect on total project costs and
would not be further discussed. Engineering details and cost estimates can be found in the
consultant's reports.
64.         Economic Analysis. The economic analysis shows a 12 percent ERR,
assuming unaccounted for water (UAW) of 20 percent, and an economic price for water
of Y 1.00lm3 (the calculations are presented in Table 6). The utility reports UAW at
10 percent, and, indeed, losses in Chinese systems from malfunctioning meters or
unreported or unmetered connections are low by international standards. However, the
utility does not have a leak detection and repair effort aggressive enough to validate the
10 percent UAW, and the 20 percent rate was selected to be in line with experience in



- 171-                        ANNE  14
other areas with low commercial losses. The economic value of water was based on the
observation that under the current price regime, with an industrial tariff of Y 0.84/r3,
industrial demand continues to exceed use quotas. On the other hand, the response to the
recently established Y 1.5/m3 price for service industry water suggests that, were that
higher price more generally applied, the water company would have a water surplus.
Given this evidence, an economic value of Y 1.00/r3 was felt to be a conservative estimate
of the true value of water.
65.         RIsks. Operational risks are held at a minimum by the fact that the water
company has operated fields using identical technologies for some decades. Because of
extensive use of the aquifers, their geology is well understood and recharge rates could be
conservatively estimated. The most substantial risk would appear to be from pollution of
the aquifer reducing the useful life of the field. The Jincheng paper mill presents that
threat, although test wells have shown no evidence of Jincheng pollution infiltrating the
field. Pollution abatement at the paper mill is funded under this project and would
substantilly reduce whatever risk does arise from -hat source. The city would have to
carefuly monitor future industrial development upstream of the weilfield for pottially
damaging pollutants.
F. AIR QUALM MANAGEMENT-BENX
66.         Background. Benxi residents suffer from some of the most severe air
pollution in the world. The 1990 base year annual average SO2 concentration reached
0.16 mg/mr, a figure far in excess of the WHO standard of 0.06 mg/rn considered safe
for long-term human exposure. Moreover, SO2 concentrations during the winter months
range between 0.42 and 0.54 mg/mr. While the WHO standard for particulate is
0.09 mg/rn, the corresponding 1990 Benxi concentration was 0.39 mg/r3, ranging up to
0.66 mg/rn during the winter months in one city district.
67.         Aware of the damaging effects of these levels of pollution, Benxi adopted
a seven-year particulate abatement program for the cement industry (which contributed
94,000 tons/year of particulate, or one third of the total) in 1988 and an overall air
pollution abatement plan in August 1992. The latter plan specifies ambient particulate and
SO2 goals for 1995 and 2000, financed through a Y 700 million investment program.
Proposed interventions include substitution in residential use of coal gas and briquettes for
raw coal, increased use of cental heating, afforestation, and control of industrial SO2
emissions. lnitial results from the cement program have reduced particulate output there
by 63,000 tons/year (and dust fall in the city from a 1980 level of 110 tons/km2/year to
30 tons/km2/year in 1992); and from the larger program include the fitting of flue gas
electrostatic precipitators to the BENGANG power plant, reducing particulates from
27,400 tons/year to 2,400 tons/year and Benxi overall particulate discharges by more than
10 percent. A coke oven gas desulfurization plant to be commissioned in 1994 should
reduce SO2 by another 8,000 tonslyear, or 5 percent of all S02 emissions. Programs are
in place to further reduce SO2 from BENGANG by 60,000 tons/year by 2000.



- 172 -                       ANNEX 14
68.         Bank assistance was sought for expanding residential use of gas and central
heating and for an innovative program to retrofit coke ovens to reduce highly carcinogenic
fugitive emissions. As components of the broader program of emission controls, these
elements hold the particular attraction of reducing ground-level emissions, those that most
immediately affect householders and workers. The gas and central heating programs
rep1ace indoor stoves used for both cooking and heat, or, in the case of apartment
dwellers, stoves and building or district boilers that operate at low efficiency and with no
emission controls. The coke oven work reduces a substantial occupational health threat
with a technology more promising than the earlier control mechanisms tested in China-
most of which have failed.
Coke Oven Fugitive Emission Control
69.         Background. Coke oven fugitive emissions of benzopyrene and other highly
carcinogenic organic chemicals have not been subject to dose-response analysis, but of 100
coke oven workers at Benxi L-on and 1'eel Company who died from other than accidental
causes between 1957 and 1979, 50 died of cancer. A 1978 Chinese national study of coke
oven workers showed that they are 8.5 times more likely to develop cancer (primarily lung
cancer) than are other metallurgical industry workers. The US Environmental Protection
Agency has labeled coke oven emissions as the most hazardous industrial waste gases. All
of these findings argue for aggressive control of coke oven emissions. However, in the
absence of dose-response functions, it would not be possible to estimate the expected
number of averted deaths from alternative levels of protection. The problem was therefore
reduced to that of identifying the least-cost technology that would meet abatement
requirements.
70.         Economic Analysis.  The Benxi EPB has mandated a reduction of
98 percent of the current coke oven emissions. This high level of reduction necessitates
a combination of a sophisticated charging car design coupled with pushing emission
controls and redesign of the quenching towers. Few technical altenatives exist that meet
the criteria. The details of, and least-cost justification for, the particular approach chosen
can be found in the consultant's report.
71.         Risks. The technology chosen has been in use in Shanghai since 1986,
although there on a newly constructed coking oven, not as a retrofit. Familiarity with the
technology within China should provide valuable backup to the project engineers during
both construction and operation. The system would require high levels of attention to
proper operation and maintenance, although that would be aided by the strong worker
interest in plovidng a more comfortable and safer working envirment.
Central Heating
72.         Background. Benxi housing is heated through one of three sources: steam
drawn from industral boilers, heating approximately 3.2 million i2; steam from single-
purpose boilers supplying entire neighborhoods, heating approximately 600,000 en; steam
from individual apartment house boilers, heating approximately 5.6 million m2; and



- 173 -                       ANNEX 14
individual household stoves heating about 10,000 units. The neighborhood and building
boilers are inefficient and lack pollution control devices. Coal-stove heating of households
leads to high levels of indoor pollution and very inefficient combustion of very low-quality
coal. The existing boilers cannot be economically retrofitted with pollution control
equipment; nor, of course, can stoves. Although a program to upg-ade coal quality would
yield substantial benefits in pollution reduction with the existing boiler and stove stock, and
remains a long-term goal supported by technical assistance work complementary to the
proposed loan, it would require a difficult provincial restructuring of the coal market. A
research program funded through trust funds would begin in 1994 to address just that
issue.
73.         Economic Analysis. In the absence of radical provincial changes in coal
policy, Benxi considered as a base case expansion of the current building/neighborhood
boiler system to replace individual stoves still in use. While modestly reducing air
pollution and substantially increasing the amenity value of converted housing, such
expansion of the current technology would continue the relatively high levels of pollution
from small boilers. Other alternatives include moving to district heating, with heat
provided by high efficiency heat-only boilers (HOBs), and district heating through a
combined heat and power (CHP) system. The latter alternative depends on heat from a
power plant, supplemented by HOBs to meet peak demand. Either of the district heating
options would displace over 9,000 of the 10,000 individual stoves, 17 of the neighborhood
boilers and 9 of the very small building boilers. Particulate reduction has been estimated
at 25,576 tons/year (11.1 percent of all Benxi particulates) and SO2 reduction at
8,416 tons/year (5.8 percent of all SO2).
74.         The analysis of the above heating options for Benxi parallels that for the
same set of options in the Beijing Environrment Project (Credit 2312-CHA/Loan 3415-
CHA). The calculation worksheets for the average incremental cost (AIC) of heat supply
can be found in the Project File. They show little difference in the between the HOB only
and CHP/HOB options, with HOB only costing an estimated Y 41.9/GJ supplied and
CHP/HOB Y 39. 1/GJ supplied. Because the timing and inputs to both construction and
operation of the two processes are similar, sensitivity analysis on fuel price changes and
other operating and mainter..nce costs, and on changes in the relative prices of construction
material, show the choice to be insensitive to possible changes in those factors. The
CHP/HOB option was therefore chosen.
75.        Risks. As with the other components of the Liaoning Environmental
Project, other cities in China use the proposed technology. Thus, plant engineers have
ready access to Chinese language material on construction and operations and can easily
and cheaply engage consultants to help solve any problems that may arise. Operational
risks are therefore minimized.
76.         A risk does arise when considering affordability. The proposed heating
tariffs average about Y 400 per family per heating season, or well above the average Y 50
per year that the lowest 5 percent urban income families in Liaoning use for electricity,
water and fuel. However, this apparent issue of affordability on closer analysis turns out



-174 -                        ANNEX 14
to be one of risk to housing privatization rather than to the project component itself. The
housing market in Benxi, as in urban Liaoning and China as a whole, is dominated by
enterprise or municipal government owned apartments. Residents of those buildings do
not pay for central heating. This contrasts with water and electricity, which are both
metered and charged to households. The central heating system financed under this project
would replace heat sources for which residents do not pay (or would provide free heat for
those houses previously using stoves, fuel for which was often highly subsidized), nor
would they pay for project-provided heat. The affordability issue therefore does not arise
at the household level. Building n- ners would face, on average, a doubling of the heating
charge, thus further increasing tile housing subsidy already provided to urban residents.
77.         The fact that central heatinp costs are borne by building owners and not
reflected in building rents suggests an extra c>.allenge faced in housing privatization efforts
in north China. After privatization, when cnterprises no longer own apartments, it would
be difficult for them to justify paying the heating bills for those apartments. Efforts to
evaluate the true costs that would be faced by private apartment owners must capture this
important additional expense.
Blast Furnace Fmission Control-Town Gas Production
78.         Background. The refitting of BENGANG Xihu number 1 and 2 blast
furnaces with electrostatic precipitators in place of wet scrubbers would free up a
signitirant quantity of coke oven gas for municipal use by substituting cleaner blast furnace
gas having a higher sensible heat content for process consumption within the Xihu factory
area. It would also eliminate the annual discharge of 4.2 million tons of highly polluted
scrubber wastewater that contains an estimated 4.7 tons of cyanide and 2 tons of phenol
and would reduce carbon monoxide emissions. There would be little direct effect on
BENGANG particulate and SO2 emissions, but the town gas, supplied would displace coal
and coke in household use, reducing particulates by some 16,400 tons/year and S02 by
1,800 tons per year.
79.         Economic Analysis.  The estimated rate of return for the proposed
investment is 6 percent (see Table 7). The economic benefits of this activity are of five
types. First, there would be savings in water and electricity use compared to the current
technology. Second, significant quantities of iron dust would be recovered. Third,
damage to the environment previously experienced through untreated wastewater flows
would end. Fourth, town gas would be made available. And, fifth, the coke and coal
consumption displaced by the town gas would result in a significant decrease in particulate
and S02 production. The water and electricity savings were valued based on the economic
price of those two services in Benxi. Iron dust was valued at market.
80.        The averted environmental damage of the wastewater was estimated by
proxy as the cost of the secondary treatment that would be needed to remove the solids and
phenols from the wastewater. It does not reflect the additional cost of removing the
cyanide. The cost of secondary treatment was estimated based on the unit cost of
secondary ticatment from large-scale plants in China, so is a lower bound to what would



-175 -                        ANNEX 14
be required for a small-scale, purpose-built plant. The preferred measure would be direct
estimation of the environmental damage, which could be lower than, or much higher than,
the cost of avoiding the camage. Such estimates are difficult to make, especially for this
one source among many affecting the receiving waters. The proposed proxy is advanced,
not because it can accurately estimate environmental damage but because it reflects the cost
of avoiding that damage, an investment that the Benxi government now deems worthwhile.
81.         The town gas is valued at its sales price. This price is used because it is a
market-clearing price for current gas production and households not yet served (a
sufficiently large number to absorb all additional production at current prices) have
indicated a strong desire to receive such services. The current price can therefore be
accepted as a lower bound of the economic value of the gas.
82.         The averted environmental damage from reduced particulate and SO2 output
due to the substitution of gas for coke and coal in the home has not been given a value.
Although work has been done that allows estimates of such damage in Benxi, it was felt
that hi this case a substantial danger of double counting exists. That danger arises because
much of the pollution directly affects the household generating it through use of coke and
ooal their stoves. The willingness-to-pay for town gas can therefore be expected to reflect,
in part, the household relief from that pollution. Of course, the positive effects of
switching to town gas benefit those outside of the household and therefore (assrming that
altruism does not sufficiently alter the household demand fully to reflect externalities) the
household willingness-to-pay does not fully capture the benefit. This simply reinforces the
prior observation that the price of town gas is a lower bound on its economic benefit.
83.         The true economic rate of return is likely to be well above the estimated
6 percent due to the consistent use of lower-bound estimates for benefits and upper-bound
estimates for costs. In addition to the instances already noted, the benefits do not include
the averted maintenance costs from the wet scrubber system (although they do include
savings in water and power). The cost estimates include the full physical contingency on
all items. For the latter, then, true base costs up to 15 percent above the estimates would
have no impact on the analysis.
84.        Risks. The greatest risk in the proposed investment would arise from a
failure of the Benxi Gas Supply Company to complete its expansion plans as scheduled.
The revenue from gas sales provides an unusual incentive to properly operate the
precipitators and without that revenue, BENGANG could lower production costs by
shutting them down (although that would be a violation of law). The Gas Supply Company
has a two-decade record of successful operations and recent increases in their tariffs have
restored profitability. Their expansion plan appears to be well within their institutional and
financial capabilities and consumer demand is weU established.
85.         Tectlnical risks do not appear abnormal. The technology has been used
elsewhere, electrostatic precipitators are already in use in the BENGANG power plant, and
the company has a large cadre of sophisticated E&M engineers.



- 176 -                        ANNEX 14
G. SOLID WASTE MANAGEMENT
86.         Background. Dalian lacks, as do all other Liaoning cities, a sanitary
landfill. That lack results in uncontrolled leachate dispersion into groundwater or coastal
waters and direct dispersion into the atmosphere of methane, an important greenhouse gas.
Leachate from the present coastal landfill site contributes to the eutrophication of Suoyu
Bay, the most polluted part of Dalian Bay. Other solid waste-handling problems include
traffic congestion from an inefficient transfer station and transport system. The cost of the
latter problem is high in Dalian's highly congested traffic network.
87.         Economic Analysis. The landfill site is already in use, but the consultants
did investigate alternative locations. That investigation concluded that the current site
offered the least costly combination of transport time and environmental vulnerability.
Design alternatives for the sit: were based on different materials to control and collect
leachate, including impermeable clay layers and artificial membrane lining. The clay
barrier was selected as the least-cost alternative, augmented by a piped leachate collection
system, storage ponds ar.d recycling leachate back into the landfill (details may be found
in the consultant's report on file). The release of "greenhouse gases' would be reduced
by using extraction wells linked to pipes, blowers, and burners to flare the gas. The
system has been designed to allow future integration into an electrical generation system
once gas volume justifies that investment.
88.         Transfer station dusign altematives are linked to transportation constraints,
in this case of a maximum 20-ton load for trucks. Five alternative transfer station/
transport models were developed and are fully described in the consultant's report. The
choices represent different combinations of truck size and tipping floor versus containerized
waste handling, with compaction included in two alternatives. For these alternatives, the
average cost per ton handled ranged from a low of Y 22.1 per ton to a high of Y 28.0 per
ton. The selected alternative uses a three-level transfer station with a storage pit, loading
uncompacted waste to 20-ton transfer trucks. The cost of this alternative is estimated at
Y 22.8 per ton, slightly higher than the lowest cost alternative. This choice was made
because in contrast to the 3 percent less costly, two-level, station, this alternative allows
separation of district waste collection trucks from transfer trucks via a storage pit that can
be isolated to control dust and pests.
89.         Risks. The recommended alternatives use technologies widely implemented
elsewhere without notable operational difficulties. The current system utlizes transfer
stations and a hauling system differing from the proposed system primarily in detail rather
than concept. The landfill leachate and gas control is successfully used elsewhere in
China. The main risks associated with the improved solid waste management system
reside in proposed organizational and management innovations, rather than hi physical
operations. The project and subsequent transfer station and landfill operations would be
managed by an independent, self-financed company rather than the govemment bureau the
now provides these service. The company viability would be threatened were the
municipal government to block the introduction of institutional and administrative
independence or the proposed tariff changes. Furthermore, company staff would be able



- 177-                        ANNEX-14
to make the best use of their new powers only if trained in their possibilities. To minimize
the tariff risk, implementation of new tariffs has been agreed by the Dalian municipal
government, with introduction slated on June 1, 1994. Approval of any further increases
needed in 1995 would be a condition of effectiveness and maintenance of financial viability
would be covenanted in the loan. The technical assistance package contains a staff training
program for the solid waste company. Successful negotiations between the project office
and the preferred finn to manage that portion of the technical assistance would be a
condition of loan negotiation, minimizing the risk of delay in implementing the technical
assistance.
R. ENVIRONMTAL REBANIUTATION AND CONSRUCTON
90.         Background. Responsibility for the protection of cultural heritage-termed
cultural relics in China-is divided among national, provincial, and local-level agencies
according to the category of the relic. Traditionally, this responsibility has been carried
out with small budgets and little coordination among line agencies. However, awareness
of environmental issues now provides an opportunity to bring heritage conservation into
the national development agenda. This has become critical, for in Liaoning Province, and
in China in general, heritage protection has been equated with preservation of individual
monuments. Little attention has been paid to the physical setting and context of historic
structures. As a resu!*, the historic fabric has deteriorated rapidly. Witness the Imperial
Palace in Shenyang, whose integrity has been severely compromised by the adjacent
construction of a large, multistory shopping complex. As land markets become more
active and property prices in the centers of historic cities increase, the threat to heritage
would also intensify.
91.         Justification. Bureaus charged with heritage conservation have left largely
unexplored the use of financial instruments to fund their activities. At this critical juncture
in the move to a market-based economy, conservation activities would survive only if a
means to maximize revenue generation can be identified. Experience elsewhere has shown
that willingness to pay depends largely on offering the public quality presentation and
interpretation of archaeological and historical sites and museums.  Based also on
international experience, visits to cultural sites can be expected to rise with the rpid
increases in discretionary income and leisure time now enjoyed in China.
92.         The project is aimed at furthering scientifically sonmd, least-cost approaches
to heritage conservation. By promoting improved management, conservation, and financial
analysis, the project would encourage more efficient allocations of human and financial
resources. The contribution of cultural heritage to domestic and international tourism in
Liaoning, would be strengthened by the project activities that address presentation of
heritage sites and museum collections. Once these improvements are in place, pricing
policies for heritage sites can be evaluated so as to increase revenue. The project, in
supporting cultural heritage conservation, would also underline the importance of this
resource to Liaoning and the nation.



- 178 -                       ANNEX 14
93.         Project elements were chosen from a broad array of possible activities, with
the selection designed to cover representative conservation and management problems in
museums, archaeological sites, and historic structures. The component has four major
elements, each with associated training: improvement of services for the world-class
collection of the Liaoning Provincial Museum; protection of the Niuheliang archeological
site of the Hongshan culture; protection and presentation of the World Heritage Site at
Jiumenkou on the Great Wall; and site investigations at the Jieshi Palace, reputed to have
been occupied by one of China's greatest emperors. The experience gained from each
activity, reinforced through training provided under the project, would be replicable in
other Liaoning sites.
